Resolving the Objection: Two Enduring Things by One Baqa'?
Objection and answer about baqa' shared by two things, with kawn analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that two enduring things cannot share one baqa' (duration) by using the analogy of kawn.

Core Idea
Two things can share an attribute only if one is the attribute itself.

After arguing that kawn is self-subsisting, the author anticipates an objection: if baqa' (duration) is a property of an essence, it cannot also be that essence itself, because that would entail two enduring things (the essence and the baqa') sharing one baqa', which is impossible. The author provides a nuanced answer, distinguishing between cases where one of the enduring things is itself the baqa'.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector states that if baqa' is made for the essence, it cannot be baqa' for itself, otherwise there would be two enduring things (the essence and the baqa') sharing one baqa'.
The objection uses the example of two movers sharing one motion.
2
Understand the author's distinction
The author explains that the impossibility only arises when both enduring things are not themselves the baqa' and require an external baqa'. If one is baqa' for itself, then no problem.
Pay attention to 'إذا كان أحد الباقيين يكون بقاء لنفسه'.
3
Connect with the kawn analogy
The author compares this to the kawn of a body: the body is 'being' by kawn, and that kawn itself is 'being' by itself, and that is not impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مختصا بهذا المكان المتعين ، وهذا لكونه معنى زائدا على ذات الجسم غير راجع إلى الذات فإنه كان موجودا قبل هذا ولا تمكن له بهذا المكان، ثم يزول التمكن وذاته موجود والعرض وهو السواد القائم بمحل كائن في هذا المحل المخصوص مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به، فإن الله تعالى كان عالما بهذا السواد في حالة العدم، وكان قادرا أن يوجده في محل آخر فإذا اختص بهذا مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به كان كائنا ولابد من أن يكون كائنا بكونه، واستحال قيام كون هو غير ذاته بذاته فكان كائنا بكون هو نفسه، وكذا كون الجسم في مكان كان كائنا في الجسم لقيامه به مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به ولا وجه إلى القول بكونه كائنا بكون قائم به فكان قائما بكون هو نفسه، إذ محال أن يكون كائنا بلا كون فكذا هذا، والإيضاح بنظير الكون للأجسام والأعراض، وإثبات كون العرض راجعا إلى ذاته. وذهب إليه بعض أصحاب الصفات منهم أبو إسحاق الأسفراييني ذكره في كتابه المسمى بتعجيز المعتزلة وغيره من الكتب وبدون الإيضاح بالنظير يثبت صحة هذا الكلام لما مر من قيام الأدلة المثبتة لذلك. ولا يقال بأن البقاء إذا جعل بقاء للذات يستحيل أن يكون بقاء لنفسه، لأنه يؤدي إلى القول بحصول الباقيين ببقاء واحد وهو محال، فإن حصول متحركين بحركة واحدة وأسودين بسواد واحد محال لأن نقول بأن حصول باقيين ببقاء واحد إنما يستحيل إذا كان أحد الباقيين مما يستحيل أن يكون بقاء لنفسه ولا يصير باقيا إلا بقيام البقاء به، وقيام بقاء واحد بذاتين محال، فاستحال صيرورتهما باقيين ببقاء واحد، فأما إذا كان أحد الباقيين يكون بقاء لنفسه ثم يقول بالباقي الآخر، كان كل واحد منها باقيا ولم يستحل ذلك، لأنه لم يؤد إلى قيام بقاء بذاتين وهو علة الاستحالة واعتبر بكون الجسم فإن الجسم يكون به كائنا والكون نفسه يكون كائنا بكون هو نفسه ولم يكن ذلك محال وكذا هذا، ويعرف ذلك بضرورة الدليل.
Specified to this particular place, and this is because it is an additional meaning to the essence of the body not reducible to the essence, because it existed before this and there is no possibility for it in this place, then the possibility ceases while its essence exists. The accident, which is blackness subsisting in a subject, is 'being' (kā'in) in this particular subject with the possibility that it is not specified to it. For God was knowing of this blackness in the state of non-existence, and was able to bring it into existence in another subject. So when it becomes specified to this [subject] with the possibility that it is not specified to it, it is 'being' (kā'in), and it must be that it is 'being' by its 'being' (kawn). It is impossible for a 'being' (kawn) that is other than its essence to subsist in its essence, so it is 'being' by a 'being' that is itself. Likewise, the 'being' (kawn) of a body in a place: the body is 'being' in it because of its subsistence in it, with the possibility that it is not specified to it, and there is no way to say that it is 'being' by a 'being' subsisting in it, so it is subsistent by a 'being' that is itself, for it is impossible for something to be 'being' without a 'being'. So it is with this. Clarification by the analogue of 'being' (kawn) for bodies and accidents, and proving that the 'being' of an accident is reducible to its essence. Some of the masters of attributes held this, among them Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, as mentioned in his book titled 'Ta'jiz al-Mu'tazila' and other books. Even without clarification by analogue, the correctness of this speech is established by what has passed of the proofs establishing that. And it should not be said that when al-baqā' (duration/remaining) is made a duration for the essence, it is impossible for it to be a duration for itself, because that leads to saying that two enduring things (bāqiyān) exist by one duration, which is impossible. For the existence of two moving things by one motion and two black things by one blackness is impossible. Because we say that the existence of two enduring things by one duration is only impossible if one of the two enduring things is such that it is impossible for it to be a duration for itself and it only becomes enduring by the subsistence of duration in it, and the subsistence of one duration in two essences is impossible. So their becoming two enduring things by one duration is impossible. However, if one of the two enduring things is a duration for itself, and then one speaks of the other enduring thing, each one of them is enduring and that is not impossible, because it does not lead to the subsistence of one duration in two essences, which is the cause of impossibility. Consider by analogy the 'being' (kawn) of the body: the body is 'being' by it, and the 'being' itself is 'being' by a 'being' that is itself, and that was not impossible. And this is similar. And that is known by the necessity of proof.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage presents a complex argument about the nature of 'being' (kawn) and 'remaining' (baqā'). The author begins by discussing an accident (like blackness) that is 'specified' (mukhtaṣṣ) to a particular subject. He argues that the blackness's 'being in that subject' is an additional meaning (ma'nā) beyond its essence, but it is not reducible to its essence because it could have been in another place. However, when it is in that particular place, it is 'being' (kā'in) by a 'being' (kawn) that is itself, because it is impossible for a being that is not itself to subsist in it. The same applies to a body's being in a place: the body's spatial existence is due to a 'being' that is itself, not a separate accident. Then the author mentions that Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held this view and that it can be established without the analogy. Next, he addresses an objection: if durance (baqā') is made a durance for the essence, it cannot be a durance for itself, because that would imply two enduring things sharing one durance, which is impossible (like two moving things sharing one motion). The answer is that this impossibility only occurs when both enduring things are not durance by themselves and require an external durance subsisting in them. But if one of them is durance for itself, then the other can be enduring by that same durance without it subsisting in two essences. He uses the analogy of 'being' (kawn): the body is 'being' by a kawn, and that kawn itself is 'being' by itself, and that is not impossible. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqā')
ذات (dhāt)
كون (kawn)
متحرك (mutaḥarrik)
أسود (aswad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if two things share one attribute, it is always impossible, like two moving things sharing one motion.
Correction: The author clarifies that this is only impossible when neither thing is that attribute itself. If one is the attribute, they can share it without the attribute subsisting in two subjects.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when is it not impossible for two enduring things to share one baqa'?
A) When both things are created
B) When one of the enduring things is itself the baqa'
C) When the baqa' is an accident
D) When the baqa' subsists in both essences

Key Takeaway
Two things can share a single attribute if one of them is that attribute itself, avoiding the impossibility of one attribute subsisting in two subjects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever claim to possess a quality (like patience, knowledge, or faith) that you actually only derive from Allah? How does the lesson's distinction between self-subsisting and dependent attributes challenge you to acknowledge your dependence?
?
When you share a characteristic with another person, do you assume the source is external to both? How does the principle that one thing can be the attribute itself change how you see the source of shared virtues in your community?
?
The objector assumed impossibility without considering the case where one thing is the attribute. In your life, have you ever dismissed a possibility too quickly because you assumed a shared attribute required an external source? How can you apply the lesson's logic to trust in Allah's self-subsistence?

Refuting the Idea that God's Knowledge is Knowing by Itself
Refutes the claim that God's knowledge could be a knower by itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi tradition holds that God's attribute of knowledge cannot be described as 'knowing' by itself, based on the incompatibility of life and knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot know itself because it lacks life – only living beings are knowers.

In this passage, the author anticipates an objection: if God's knowledge is 'knowledge for itself', wouldn't it be a knower? The answer is a powerful demonstration of rational consistency – showing that knowledge, as an attribute, cannot possess life, and thus cannot be a knowing entity. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications for how we understand divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening lines where the opponent argues that if knowledge is for itself, it would be knowing.
Notice the conditional 'if' structure.
2
Understand the first part of the answer
The author says knowledge cannot be conceived as living, so it cannot be a knower. Grasp why life is a prerequisite for knowledge.
This is a key rational principle.
3
Examine the second argument
The author adds that if knowledge were for itself, it would have two incompatible descriptions (remaining and knowing) for the same thing.
This shows logical contradiction.
4
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how these two reasons together block the objection and preserve the doctrine that God alone is the Knower.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لو جاز هذا الجاز أن يكون علم الله تعالى علما له ولنفسه، فيكون الذات به عالما وهو أيضا بنفسه عالم والجواب عنه أن هذا محال، لأن العلم لا يتصور أن يكون حيا فلا يتصور أن يكون عالما، لاستحالة عالم لا حياة له بخلاف البقاء. ولأنا إذا عرفنا بضرورة الدليل أن العلم بقاء لنفسه لم يجز أن يكون علما لنفسه لأنه لو كان كذلك لاستفاد العلم وصفين مختلفين، وهما كونه باقيا عالما بشيء واحد وهو محال.
If they say: 'If this [kind of] permissibility is allowed, then the knowledge of God would be knowledge for Him and for itself, so the essence would be knowing by it and it itself would also be knowing by itself.' The answer is that this is impossible, because knowledge cannot be conceived to be living, so it cannot be conceived to be knowing, because it is impossible for something without life to be knowing, unlike al-baqā'. And because if we know by necessity of proof that knowledge is a remaining (baqā') for itself, it is not permissible for it to be knowledge for itself, because if that were the case, knowledge would acquire two different descriptions: its being remaining and its being knowing of one thing, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage presents an objection: if God's knowledge is said to be 'knowledge for itself', then it would be both a source of knowledge for God and itself knowing. The author replies that this is impossible because knowledge is not alive (ḥayy), and only living things can be knowing. So knowledge cannot be 'ālim (knowing). Furthermore, knowledge is baqā' for itself (it endures by itself), but if it were also knowledge for itself, it would have two incompatible attributes: being enduring (bāqī) and being knowing ('ālim). A single thing cannot have two different descriptions that are incompatible, so the objection fails.

علم ('ilm)
حياة (ḥayāh)
عالم ('ālim)
بقاء (baqā')
ذات (dhāt)
محال (muḥāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if knowledge is 'knowledge for itself', it automatically becomes knowing.
Correction: The author clarifies that knowledge lacks life, so it cannot be a knower.

Checkpoint

A) Because knowledge cannot be a knower since it is not alive
B) Because knowledge is not an attribute
C) Because God alone is knowing
D) Because knowledge is eternal and cannot change

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is an attribute that subsists in His essence, but it does not possess life, so it cannot be a knower—only Allah, who is living, is the Knower.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The opponent imagined that an attribute could act independently. Is there an area of your life where you treat Allah's blessings (like health, wealth, or intellect) as if they have power on their own, separate from Him? Commit today to one habit that reminds you: all qualities depend on the Living, Knowing Allah alone.

Objection and Answer on Knowledge as Baqa' for Itself
Objection and answer on knowledge as baqa' for itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection against knowledge as baqa' for itself and the author's precise distinction that resolves the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
Knowledge is 'ilm for the essence but baqa' for itself—no circularity.

This lesson addresses a subtle objection raised against the thesis that divine knowledge is baqa' (remaining) for itself. Opponents argue that this leads to an impossible circularity. The author provides a precise distinction to show why the objection fails, clarifying the different respects in which knowledge is 'ilm and baqa'.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the opponents construct the impossibility: if knowledge is baqa' for itself, then the essence would be knowing by that which is baqa', and knowledge would be remaining by that which is more knowing? They claim this is also impossible.
2
Analyze the author's reply
The author clarifies that the impossibility would only arise if knowledge were knowledge for that which is baqa' for it and baqa' for that which is knowledge for it. But the actual case is different.
Pay attention to the conditional 'if' in the reply.
3
Grasp the key distinction
The author states that knowledge is 'ilm (knowledge) for the essence (making it knowing) but not baqa' for the essence; and it is baqa' for itself (enduring by itself) but not knowledge for itself. This avoids circularity.
The distinction lies in the subject of attribution: essence vs. itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: هذه الاستحالة ثابتة ها هنا فإن العلم لما كان بقاء لنفسه كان الذات عالما بما هو بقاء والعلم بنفسه باقيا بما هو أعلم أو هذا محال أيضا. قيل : إنما يكون ذلك محالا إذ لو كان العلم علما لما هو بقاء له وبقاء لما هو علم له، وليس الأمر كذلك بل هو علم للذات وليس ببقاء له وبقاء لنفسه وليس بعلم له.
If they say: 'This impossibility is established here, because when knowledge is a remaining for itself, the essence is knowing by what is remaining, and knowledge itself is remaining by what is more knowing? Or this is also impossible.' It is said: That is only impossible if knowledge were knowledge for what is a remaining for it and a remaining for what is knowledge for it. But the matter is not so; rather, it is knowledge for the essence and not a remaining for it, and a remaining for itself and not knowledge for it.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
The opponents press further: if knowledge is baqā' (remaining) for itself, then the divine essence would be knowing by that which is a baqā', and knowledge would be remaining by that which is more knowing? This is also impossible. The author replies that this would be impossible only if knowledge were knowledge for that which is a baqā' for it, and baqā' for that which is knowledge for it. But this is not the case. Rather, knowledge is 'ilm for the essence (it makes the essence knowing) but it is not baqā' for the essence; and it is baqā' for itself (it endures by itself) but it is not knowledge for itself. So there is no circularity or impossible combination.

Key Terms
علم (ʿilm)
بقاء (baqāʾ)
ذات (dhāt)
محال (muḥāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If knowledge is both 'ilm (making knowing) and baqā' (remaining), it cannot be consistently attributed to the same entity.
Correction: The contradiction only arises if it were both 'ilm and baqā' for the same subject. In fact, it is 'ilm for the essence and baqā' for itself, so no contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is there no contradiction when knowledge is both 'ilm for the essence and baqā' for itself?
A) Because knowledge is not really baqā'
B) Because the two descriptions apply to different subjects: knowledge is 'ilm for the essence and baqā' for itself
C) Because the essence can be knowing without life
D) Because baqā' and 'ilm are the same thing

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is 'ilm for the essence but baqā' for itself; by distinguishing the subjects of attribution, the alleged impossibility is resolved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own attributes—like your knowledge or your patience—do you ever confuse them, thinking you must be one thing for everyone? The passage shows that knowledge can be for the essence without being for itself. Where in your life do you need to accept that you can be something for God (like a servant) but not for yourself, to avoid inner contradiction? What one relationship today needs this clarity?
?
The objector assumed a contradiction where none existed. In your spiritual life, do you ever assume that two truths about Allah (like His mercy and His justice) are contradictory? How does the logical tool of distinguishing respects help you trust in the coherence of Allah's attributes? What doubt about Allah's perfection can you lay aside today?
?
Knowledge is described as remaining for itself—it does not need anything else to endure. How does your own sense of worth or identity depend on others? Are you living as if your 'remaining' depends on something other than the One who is self-subsistent? What practical step can you take today to anchor your value in Allah alone, not in what you know or what others think of you?

Refuting the Claim That Life Must Be Knowing
Rejects the argument that life must be knowing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of life (ḥayāh) cannot be said to be knowing, based on the argument that it is a surviving attribute for itself.

Core Idea
Life endures by itself, so it cannot be 'knowing' in the way a subject knows.

In this passage, the author continues the pattern of refuting arguments that confuse the nature of divine attributes. The opponents claim that because life makes the essence alive, it should itself be knowing. But the author shows this is incoherent: life's self-subsistence prevents it from being a subject that possesses knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic and its English translation to get the gist of the argument.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The opponents claim that life should be knowing because it is life for the essence and knowledge for itself.
This sets up the refutation.
3
Understand the refutation
The author argues that since life is a remaining (baqā') for itself, it cannot be conceived to be life for itself, hence it cannot be knowing.
The reasoning mirrors earlier arguments about other attributes.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Reflect on how this refutation fits the pattern: attributes are not subjects that can possess other attributes.
This is a key principle in Maturidi theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهكذا الجواب عن قولهم - إن الحياة ينبغي أن تكون عالمة لأنها تكون حياة للذات علم لنفسها - لأنها لما كانت بقاء لنفسها لا يتصور أن تكون حياة لنفسها
And likewise the answer to their saying: 'Life should be knowing, because it is life for the essence and knowledge for itself' — because when it is a remaining for itself, it cannot be conceived to be life for itself.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This short passage extends the previous reasoning to the attribute of life (ḥayāh). The opponents argue that life should be 'ālima (knowing) because it is life for the essence and knowledge for itself (i.e., it makes the essence alive and also itself has knowledge?). The author replies analogously: since life is baqā' (remaining) for itself (i.e., it endures by itself), it cannot be conceived to be life for itself. The reasoning mirrors the previous points: life is not a subject that can possess attributes like knowing. The answer is cut off, but the intended argument is clear from the pattern.

Key Terms
حياة (ḥayāh)
علم ('ilm)
بقاء (baqā')
عالم ('ālim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that life, because it makes the essence alive, must itself be knowing.
Correction: Life is a self-subsisting attribute (baqā') and not a subject, so it cannot possess knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that life must be knowing?
A) Because life is not an attribute
B) Because life is a remaining for itself, and it cannot be life for itself, so it cannot be knowing
C) Because life is not eternal
D) Because life and knowledge are the same

Key Takeaway
Life's self-subsistence (baqā') prevents it from being a subject that can possess knowledge; therefore, the claim that life must be knowing is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How does this lesson's refutation—that life cannot be knowing because it is self-subsisting—help you avoid such anthropomorphism? What concrete step will you take to purify your concept of Allah's attributes?
?
This lesson refutes a subtle rational argument. Are you willing to submit your own thinking to the discipline of theological study, even when it challenges your initial logic? After reading this, what preconception about Allah are you ready to let go of, and how will you seek correction from reliable sources?

Why Two Entities Cannot Share One Permanence
Argument on impossibility of shared permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two entities cannot be permanent by a single permanence and how self-subsisting permanence avoids contradiction.

Core Idea
A single attribute cannot inhere in two subjects.

This passage tackles a subtle but crucial point in kalam: can two entities share the same attribute of permanence? The author argues no, because a single permanence cannot inhere in two essences. But he then explains how self-subsisting permanence for each avoids contradiction, using an analogy from the concept of 'being'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the concise argument style.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that a single permanence (بقاء) cannot inhere in two essences (ذاتين). This is the bedrock principle.
This is the key premise for the impossibility.
3
Understand the exception
If each entity has its own self-subsisting permanence (بقاء لنفسه), then each is permanent without contradiction. This avoids the problem of a shared attribute.
The cause of impossibility is the inherence of one permanence in two subjects.
4
Analyze the analogy of kawn
The author uses the concept of 'being' (كون): the body is 'being' by a being, and that being itself is 'being' by another being. This shows that self-subsisting attributes are not impossible.
The analogy clarifies how self-subsisting permanence works.
5
Reflect on the necessity of proof
The argument concludes that this is known by the necessity of reason (ضرورة الدليل). Reflect on why this is a foundational principle in kalam.
This principle governs how attributes relate to essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
به، وقيام بقاء واحد بذاتين محال، فاستحال صيرورتهما باقيين ببقاء واحد، فأما إذا كان أحد الباقيين يكون بقاء لنفسه ثم يقول بالباقي الآخر ، كان كل واحد منها باقيا ولم يستحل ذلك، لأنه لم يؤد إلى قيام بقاء بذاتين وهو علة الاستحالة واعتبر بكون الجسم فإن الجسم يكون به كائنا والكون نفسه يكون كائنا بكون هو نفسه ولم يكن ذلك محال وكذا هذا، ويعرف ذلك بضرورة الدليل.
And it is impossible for a single permanence to inhere in two essences. So it is impossible for them both to become permanent by one permanence. But if one of the two permanent things has a permanence that is itself, and then the other permanent is posited, each of them is permanent and that is not impossible, because it does not lead to the inherence of a permanence in two essences, which is the cause of the impossibility. Consider by analogy the 'being' (kawn) of the body: the body is 'being' (ka'in) by a being (kawn), and the being itself is 'being' by a being that is itself, and that was not impossible; likewise this. And that is known by the necessity of the proof.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational argument about the nature of permanence (بقاء). The author begins by stating a general rule: it is impossible for a single permanence to inhere in two essences. Therefore, if two things were both permanent, they cannot share one and the same permanence. However, if each has its own self-subsisting permanence (بقاء لنفسه), then each is permanent without contradiction, because the problem of a single shared attribute inhering in two subjects is avoided. To illustrate, the author uses the analogy of 'being' (كون) and 'beingness' (كون) in the body. The body is 'being' (كائن) by virtue of a 'being' (كون). That 'being' itself is also 'being' by another 'being' that is itself. This is not considered impossible, and similarly, the case of two permanences is analogous. The argument relies on the principle that an attribute cannot inhere in two subjects simultaneously. The proof is known by necessity of reason (ضرورة الدليل).

Key Terms
بقاء
ذات
كون
كائن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two entities cannot share any attribute at all.
Correction: The impossibility is specifically about a single attribute inhering in two subjects simultaneously; attributes can be shared in other ways (e.g., each having its own instance).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for two entities to be permanent by a single permanence?
A) Because permanence is an attribute of God alone
B) Because it would entail a single permanence inhering in two essences
C) Because permanence is self-subsisting
D) Because both entities would need to be eternal

Key Takeaway
A single attribute cannot inhere in two different subjects; each subject must have its own attribute instance to be described by it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that a single attribute cannot inhere in two subjects. Reflect on your own relationship with Allah: if His attributes are unique and uncaused, how does this principle protect you from ever attributing His perfections to yourself? What does that demand of your heart in prayer and gratitude?
?
Consider the analogy: the body's 'being' is from a 'being' that itself is self-subsisting. Your entire existence—every attribute you have—depends on a self-subsisting Source. Do you live as if your permanence is borrowed? When you feel self-sufficient, which part of this passage could you recite to yourself to remember your utter dependence?

The Impossibility of Knowledge Being Self-Knowing
Refutation of the claim that God's knowledge could be knowledge for itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing self-knowledge to God's attribute of knowledge leads to logical impossibility.

Core Idea
Even attributes cannot be both self-subsisting and knowing.

The objection claims that if God's knowledge is a permanence for itself, it could also be knowledge for itself. Imam al-Nasafi refutes this by showing that knowledge cannot be alive, and that a single entity cannot simultaneously be both permanent and knowing. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. The objector argues that if knowledge is self-subsisting (permanence for itself), then it could also be knowledge for itself, making it a knower.
2
Understand the answer's first part
The author replies that knowledge cannot be alive, so it cannot be knowing. Notice the contrast with permanence (baqa') which does not require life.
A knower must be alive; knowledge is not alive.
3
Understand the answer's second part
Even if knowledge were self-knowing, it would acquire two incompatible attributes: being permanent and being knowing of one thing. That is impossible.
A single entity cannot have two distinct attributes simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لو جاز هذا الجاز أن يكون علم الله تعالى علما له ولنفسه، فيكون الذات به عالما وهو أيضا بنفسه عالم والجواب عنه أن هذا محال، لأن العلم لا يتصور أن يكون حيا فلا يتصور أن يكون عالما، لاستحالة عالم لا حياة له بخلاف البقاء. ولأنا إذا عرفنا بضرورة الدليل أن العلم بقاء لنفسه لم يجز أن يكون علما لنفسه لأنه لو كان كذلك لاستفاد العلم وصفين مختلفين، وهما كونه باقيا عالما بشيء واحد وهو محال.
If they say: If it is allowed that the knowledge of God Most High is a knowledge for Him and for itself, then the essence would be knowing by it and it itself would be knowing by itself. The answer is that this is impossible, because knowledge cannot be conceived as being alive, so it cannot be conceived as being knowing, due to the impossibility of a knower without life, unlike permanence. And because we know by the necessity of proof that knowledge is a permanence for itself, it is not permissible for it to be knowledge for itself, because if it were so, knowledge would acquire two different attributes: its being permanent and its being knowing of one thing, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that if God's knowledge is allowed to be a permanence for itself (as the author holds), then it would be knowledge both for God and for itself. That would mean God's essence is knowing by it, and the knowledge itself is knowing by itself. The author replies that this is impossible because knowledge cannot be conceived as alive, and a knower must be alive. Since knowledge is not alive, it cannot be knowing. This is unlike permanence, which can be self-subsisting without needing life. Furthermore, the author notes that we already know by necessity of proof that knowledge is a permanence for itself. If it were also knowledge for itself, it would acquire two different attributes (being permanent and being knowing of one thing) which is impossible for a single entity.

Key Terms
علم
بقاء لنفسه
حي
عالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is about God's knowledge being eternal.
Correction: The objection is actually about knowledge being self-subsisting (permanence for itself), not merely eternal.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that God's knowledge could be knowledge for itself?
A) Because knowledge is not eternal
B) Because knowledge cannot be alive and therefore cannot be knowing
C) Because knowledge is a substance
D) Because knowledge is not a permanent attribute

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because knowledge cannot be alive and thus cannot be a knower.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Where in your own mind have you shaped God's attributes according to created things rather than revelation? Will you actively repent of such comparisons and submit to what the Qur'an and Sunnah teach about His uniqueness?
?
The author argues that knowledge cannot be a knower because it lacks life. This reminds us that true knowing requires life from the Creator. In your pursuit of religious knowledge, do you seek merely information, or do you seek a living knowledge that transforms your heart? How will you ensure that what you learn brings you closer to the Living God rather than puffing you up?

If even an attribute of God cannot be an independent knower, how much less can you, a creature, claim self-sufficiency in knowledge or existence? In what area of your life are you acting as if you are 'knowledge for yourself'—independent of God's life-giving guidance? What concrete step will you take today to acknowledge your utter dependence on Him?

Second Objection on Knowledge and Its Answer
Answer to second objection about knowledge's self-subsistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi answer to the second objection resolves the apparent impossibility by distinguishing between knowledge being for the essence and permanence for itself.

Core Idea
Knowledge is for the essence, but permanence is for itself — this avoids the fallacy.

The second objection attempts to show that the same impossibility arises if knowledge is a self-subsisting attribute: the essence would know by something that is permanence, and knowledge would be permanent by something more knowledgeable. The answer clarifies the direction of each relation, showing that the distinction between 'being knowledge for X' and 'being permanence for X' dissolves the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection: if knowledge is a permanence for itself, then the essence knows by what is a permanence (knowledge) and knowledge itself is permanent by what is more knowledgeable. This is presented as impossible.
Notice the objector's assumption about reciprocity.
2
Identify the answer's key distinction
The answer says it would be impossible only if knowledge were knowledge for its permanence and permanence for its knowledge. But it is not so.
The key phrase: بل هو علم للذات وليس ببقاء له وبقاء لنفسه وليس بعلم له
3
Understand the two relations
Knowledge is knowledge for the essence (God's essence knows by it) but not a permanence for the essence. Knowledge is a permanence for itself (it endures by itself) but not knowledge for itself.
This prevents the absurdity of circular attribution.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
This distinction shows that the same attribute can have different relational properties toward different subjects without contradiction.
Connects to broader kalam principles about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: هذه الاستحالة ثابتة ها هنا فإن العلم لما كان بقاء لنفسه كان الذات عالما بما هو بقاء والعلم بنفسه باقيا بما هو أعلم أو هذا محال أيضا. قيل: إنما يكون ذلك محالا إذ لو كان العلم علما لما هو بقاء له وبقاء لما هو علم له، وليس الأمر كذلك بل هو علم للذات وليس ببقاء له وبقاء لنفسه وليس بعلم له.
If they say: This impossibility is established here, because when knowledge is a permanence for itself, the essence would be knowing by what is a permanence, and knowledge itself would be permanent by what is more knowledgeable? Or this is also impossible. It is said: This would only be impossible if knowledge were knowledge for what is its permanence and permanence for what is its knowledge, but that is not the case. Rather, it is knowledge for the essence and not a permanence for it, and it is a permanence for itself and not knowledge for itself.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objector argues that the same impossibility (of acquiring two different attributes) arises here: if knowledge is a permanence for itself, then the essence would be knowing by something that is a permanence (i.e., knowledge), and knowledge itself would be permanent by something that is more knowledgeable? This is also impossible. The author answers that this would be impossible only if knowledge were knowledge for that which is its permanence and permanence for that which is its knowledge. But that is not the case. Instead, knowledge is knowledge for the essence (i.e., God's essence knows by it) and it is not a permanence for the essence; it is a permanence for itself and not knowledge for itself. This distinction avoids the supposed impossibility.

Key Terms
علم
بقاء
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if an attribute is self-subsisting, then it must be both knowledge and permanence for the same subject, leading to circularity.
Correction: The answer shows that knowledge can be knowledge for one subject (the essence) and permanence for another (itself), avoiding circularity.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how is knowledge related to the essence and to itself?
A) Knowledge is knowledge for the essence and permanence for the essence
B) Knowledge is knowledge for the essence and permanence for itself
C) Knowledge is permanence for the essence and knowledge for itself
D) Knowledge is neither knowledge nor permanence for the essence

Key Takeaway
The apparent impossibility is resolved by distinguishing the direction of the attribute's relations: knowledge is for the essence, permanence is for itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's knowledge is self-subsisting and not dependent on anything else, does your trust in His decree fully reflect that? Or do you sometimes feel His knowledge must conform to your limited understanding?
?
When you face confusion about why something happens, are you tempted to measure Allah's knowledge against human logic? How can the lesson's distinction (knowledge for essence vs. permanence for itself) correct that tendency?
?
Do you ever subconsciously assume that Allah's attributes must function like human attributes? What would change in your worship if you truly internalized that His knowledge is perfect, self-subsisting, and beyond any reciprocal dependency?

Life as an Attribute: Not a Subject of Knowledge
Refutation of the claim that life is knowing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the claim that life itself must be knowing, and the reasoning that life as an attribute cannot be a subject of knowledge.

Core Idea
Life is a permanence for itself, not a living subject.

This lesson continues the discussion on divine attributes, addressing an objection about the attribute of life. The objector argues that life should be knowing because it is life for God and knowledge for itself. The author refutes this by showing that life is a permanence for itself and cannot be life for itself; otherwise it would have to be alive and then could not possess knowledge and power. The reasoning parallels the earlier discussion on knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
Read the objection: the objector claims life should be knowing because it is life for God and knowledge for itself. Understand the assumption that life can be a subject of knowledge.
2
Understand the answer
The author argues life is a permanence for itself, so it cannot be life for itself. If it were, it would be alive, and then it would be impossible for it to be powerful and knowing. The final sentence reinforces that the objector's claim is impossible.
3
Apply the principle
Notice the consistency with the earlier example of knowledge: an attribute cannot be a subject for another attribute. Life does not itself possess knowledge or power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهكذا الجواب عن قولهم إن الحياة ينبغي أن تكون عالمة لأنها تكون حياة للذات علما لنفسها لأنها لما كانت بقاء لنفسها لا يتصور أن تكون حياة لنفسها لهذا المعنى، ولأنها لو كانت حياة لنفسها لكانت حية، ويستحيل أن تكون قادرة عالمة، والقول بحي يستحيل أن يكون عالما قادرا محال
And likewise the answer to their statement that life should be knowing because it is life for the essence and knowledge for itself: Because when it is a permanence for itself, it cannot be conceived as life for itself for this reason. And if it were life for itself, it would be alive, and it is impossible for it to be powerful and knowing. The statement that a living being is impossible to be knowing and powerful is impossible.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage extends the same reasoning to the attribute of life. The objector claims that life should be knowing, since it is life for God and knowledge for itself. The author answers that life is a permanence for itself; therefore it cannot be life for itself (i.e., it does not need to be alive itself). If it were life for itself, it would be alive, and then it would be impossible for it to be powerful and knowing simultaneously. The underlying principle is that a living being cannot be both knowing and powerful? Actually the text says 'يستحيل أن تكون قادرة عالمة' (it is impossible for it to be powerful and knowing) and then 'والقول بحي يستحيل أن يكون عالما قادرا محال' (the statement that a living being is impossible to be knowing and powerful is impossible? Wait: 'محال' at the end indicates that this statement is itself impossible). The author is denying that a living being can have both attributes? That seems contradictory. Possibly the meaning: if life were alive, it would be a living entity, but a living entity cannot be knowing and powerful? Actually in Islamic theology, God is alive, knowing, powerful, so that cannot be impossible. The context might be that life itself, if considered as a living entity, cannot have the attributes of knowledge and power because those are distinct attributes. The author is arguing that life, if it were a living being, would not be knowledgeable or powerful, which would be a deficiency. Therefore it is impossible for life to be alive in that sense. The correct interpretation is that life cannot be considered a subject that has knowledge and power. So the objection fails. The answer is analogous to the previous case of knowledge.

Correction: The argument is not that life is not an attribute of God, but that life itself does not possess the attribute of knowledge. Life is a permanence for itself, not a living subject.

Checkpoint

A) Because life is a substance
B) Because life is a permanence for itself and cannot be life for itself, and if it were it would be alive but then cannot be knowledgeable
C) Because life is eternal
D) Because life is not an attribute of God

Key Takeaway
Life is an attribute of God, not a living subject; it cannot itself possess knowledge or power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Why Accidents Cannot Be Self-Subsisting Permanences
Refutation of self-subsisting permanence in accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidental attributes (a'rad) cannot be self-subsisting permanences, and how this distinction protects the eternity of God's attributes.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot be permanent for themselves; only God's attributes are eternal.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if an attribute can be a permanence (baqa'), why can't an accident like blackness be a self-subsisting permanence? The author shows why this is impossible for originated things, preserving the uniqueness of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that if the principle of self-subsisting permanence applies to attributes, then accidents like blackness would also be self-subsisting permanences. Read this objection to understand the challenge.
This objection aims to force a contradiction in the author's position.
2
Examine the author's response
The author explains that an accident cannot be a permanence for itself because every accident is originated (hadith). At the first moment of existence, it cannot be permanent since permanence requires duration, which is absent at that instant.
The key point: no duration at the first moment.
3
Contrast with God's attributes
Unlike accidents, God's attributes are not originated; they are eternal (azali). Therefore, there is no first moment where they lack permanence. The impossibility argument does not apply to them.
This contrasts the temporal with the eternal.
4
Understand the rebuttal about witnessed permanence
The objector might say that the permanence of an accident (like its duration) is itself a permanence at the first moment. The author replies: it is a permanence for the essence, not for itself. The accident's own essence is not a permanence.
This clarifies the distinction between being a permanence and having permanence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: يلزمكم على هذا الأصل القول بجواز بقاء الأعراض، فيكون كل عرض بقاء لنفسه، فيكون السواد ادا للجسم القائم به بنفسه، بقاء لنفسه قيل لهم: إن العرض لا يجوز أن يكون بقاء لنفسه، لأن كل عرض محدث وفى أول ما دخل في حيز الوجود يستحيل أن يكون باقيا، لأنه عبارة عن الدوام، ولا دوام في تلك الحالة ونفسه في تلك الحالة موجودة ولم تكن بقاء لما بينا من الاستحالة، فلم يكن باقيا ببقاء هو نفسه، لأن نفسه ليست ببقاء لأنها في تلك الحالة التي بينا موجودة ولم تكن بقاء إذ لو كانت نفسه بقاء لنفسه لكانت نفسه باقية في تلك الحالة لاستحالة حصول بقاء لا باقي به. وهذا بخلاف صفات الله تعالى فإنها لم تكن محدثة ليكون له حالة لم تكن فيها باقية ولا ذاتها كانت ببقاء بل كانت أزلية فلم يوجد فيها دليل استحالة كونها بقاء لأنفسها، وقد قام دليل كونها بقاء. وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن البقاء الشاهد ينبغي أن يكون جائز البقاء، لأنه في أول ما دخل في حيز الوجود بقاء فإنا نقول: نعم هو في تلك الحالة ولكن للذات الذي كان بقاء له فأما لنفسه فليس ببقاء للحال، لاستحالة كونه باقيا في تلك الحالة. والله الموفق.
If it is said: According to this principle, you are compelled to affirm the possibility of the permanence of accidents, so every accident would be a permanence for itself; thus blackness would be an instrument for the body subsisting by itself, a permanence for itself. They are told: An accident cannot be a permanence for itself, because every accident is originated, and at the first moment it enters existence it is impossible for it to be permanent, because permanence is an expression of duration, and there is no duration in that state. And its essence in that state is existent and was not a permanence, due to the impossibility we have explained. So it is not permanent by a permanence that is itself, because its essence is not a permanence, since it is in that state which we have explained as existent and not a permanence. For if its essence were a permanence for itself, its essence would be permanent in that state, because it is impossible for a permanence to obtain without something permanent. And this is contrary to the attributes of God Most High, for they were not originated such that there would be a state in which they were not permanent, nor was their essence with a permanence; rather they were eternal, so the proof of the impossibility of their being permanence for themselves does not apply to them, and the proof of their being permanence has been established. And by this they answer their statement: 'The witnessed permanence should be permissible of permanence, because at the first moment it enters existence it is a permanence.' For we say: Yes, it is in that state, but for the essence for which it was a permanence; as for itself, it is not a permanence at that state, due to the impossibility of its being permanent in that state. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection related to accidental attributes (الأعراض). The objector claims that if the author's principle (that an attribute can be a self-subsisting permanence) is applied to accidents, then every accident would be a permanence for itself. For example, blackness would be a self-subsistent permanence. The author responds that an accident cannot be a permanence for itself because every accident is originated (محدث). At the first moment of its existence, it cannot be permanent, because permanence entails duration, and there is no duration at that instant. Moreover, its essence at that moment exists but is not a permanence. If its essence were a permanence for itself, it would be permanent at that instant, but it is impossible to have a permanence without something that is permanent (i.e., a subject). This is contrasted with God's attributes: they are not originated, so there is no moment when they lack permanence; they are eternal. Therefore, the impossibility of self-subsisting permanence does not apply to them. The author then addresses another objection: the 'witnessed permanence' (i.e., the permanence of an accident that we observe) seems to be a permanence at the first moment. The reply is that it is a permanence at that moment, but it is a permanence for the essence (the accident itself) that it subsists, not a permanence for itself as a self-subsistent attribute. The argument concludes with 'والله الموفق' (God is the Grantor of success).

Key Terms
عرض
بقاء
محدث
أزلي
صفات الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that accidental attributes like blackness could be eternal or self-subsisting permanences.
Correction: Accidents are temporally originated; they cannot be permanent for themselves because at the first moment of existence they lack duration. Only God's attributes are eternal and self-subsisting.

Checkpoint
Why can an accidental attribute (عرض) not be a permanence for itself?
A) Because accidents are not real
B) Because accidents are originated and at the first moment they lack duration
C) Because accidents are eternal
D) Because accidents are not attributes

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot be self-subsisting permanences because they are originated and lack duration at the first moment, unlike God's eternal attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often rely on things that change and fade—your health, your income, your relationships. This lesson teaches that even their 'permanence' is not self-subsisting; they depend entirely on God. What is one such thing in your life that you have treated as if it were eternal, and what concrete step can you take today to shift your trust from that to God's eternal attributes?
?
The author argues that only God's attributes are truly eternal. Think of a time you felt your world was shaken because something you depended on failed you. How does this truth about the contingency of all created things change how you will respond to that failure spiritually? Write a short prayer acknowledging God's permanence in that area.
?
This passage distinguishes between the permanence of an accident for itself (impossible) and for another (possible). In your own spiritual life, where are you seeking permanence from something that can only provide it temporarily—like seeking lasting satisfaction in a created being? What would it look like to seek that permanence directly from God today?

Denying Two Problematic Attribute Configurations
The author clarifies two doctrines not held by the Maturidi school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two specific positions that the Maturidi school denies regarding divine attributes: a permanent without permanence and an attribute subsisting in another attribute.

Core Idea
Attributes are not separated from their subjects, nor do they inhere in other attributes.

This concluding sentence encapsulates a crucial clarification from Imam Nasafi. After establishing the Maturidi position on divine attributes, he explicitly denies two problematic configurations that critics might attribute to the school. Understanding what is denied here deepens our grasp of how attributes are affirmed without falling into anthropomorphism or philosophical absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation. Note the brevity and conclusive tone—this is a summary of preceding arguments.
The Arabic uses 'ويثبت بهذا' to show that this conclusion follows from earlier proofs.
2
Identify the two denials
The passage denies two things: (1) a permanent without permanence (باق بلا بقاء), and (2) an attribute inhering in another attribute (قيام صفة بصفة). Understand what each means.
3
Reflect on why these are problematic
Think about why each denial is necessary. A permanent without permanence would separate an entity from its defining attribute. An attribute inhering in another attribute would lead to an infinite regress or composition in the divine essence.
These are logical impossibilities in kalam.
4
Connect to the broader argument
This statement is the conclusion of a longer discussion. Consider how the previous arguments (in preceding lessons) lead to these negations.
The passage says 'by this it is established'—so the proof is in the prior text.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can distinguish what is denied from what is affirmed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويثبت بهذا أنا لم نقل بباق بلا بقاء ولا بقيام صفة بصفة.
And by this it is established that we do not affirm a permanent without permanence, nor the subsistence of an attribute by an attribute.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This is a brief concluding statement that summarizes the position established by the preceding arguments. The author asserts two negations: first, they do not hold that there is a permanent entity (باق) without a permanence (بقاء) (i.e., that an attribute of permanence inheres in a subject without the subject having a permanent attribute). Second, they do not affirm the subsistence of one attribute by another attribute (قيام صفة بصفة), meaning that one attribute does not inhere in another attribute. This directly addresses potential charges that the author's doctrine might lead to either of these problematic positions.

Key Terms
باق
بقاء
قيام صفة بصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Maturidi school denies attributes like permanence or denies that attributes can be related.
Correction: The text only denies a permanent without permanence and an attribute inhering in another attribute, not attributes themselves.

Checkpoint
What does the author affirm as established by the previous arguments?
A) That there is a permanent without permanence
B) That an attribute subsists by another attribute
C) That neither a permanent without permanence nor an attribute subsisting by an attribute is affirmed
D) That all attributes are self-subsisting

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position firmly rejects two logical impossibilities: a permanent entity without its attribute of permanence, and an attribute that subsists in another attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah as 'al-Bāqī' (the Everlasting), do you truly believe that His permanence is an attribute inseparable from His essence, or do you sometimes imagine a detached 'permanence' that exists on its own? What thought leads you to that separation, and how will you correct it?
?
If you deny that one of Allah's attributes can subsist in another, does that change how you relate to His attributes like knowledge, power, and will? Have you ever unconsciously treated one attribute as dependent on another? What would it mean for your worship if they were not all equally and simultaneously perfect?
?
The passage refutes two subtle errors about God's attributes. Which of these errors have you unknowingly fallen into when thinking about Allah? What specific step will you take today to guard your 'aqīdah against such confusions?

Objection and Answer on the Permanence of Accidents
Refutation of the claim that accidents persist by themselves, distinguishing created and divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi argument that accidents cannot persist by themselves and why divine attributes are different from created accidents.

Core Idea
An accident’s first moment of existence excludes duration, so it cannot be permanent by itself.

In Islamic theology, the nature of accidents and divine attributes is debated. This passage from Tabsirat al-Adilla presents an objection against the Maturidi position that only substances persist. The objector claims that if accidents do not persist, then they would have to be their own permanence. The author responds by clarifying the temporal status of accidents versus eternal divine attributes. Read on to see how this critical distinction is made.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues that if the author’s principle is true, then accidents would have to be self-persisting. Notice how the objector tries to force a logical consequence.
The objector says: 'You are forced by this principle to say that the permanence of accidents is permissible.'
2
Follow the author’s response
The author denies that accidents can be permanent by themselves because every accident is originated and cannot have duration in its first moment.
Key phrase: 'every accident is originated, and in the first moment it enters into existence it is impossible for it to be permanent.'
3
Distinguish divine attributes
The author explains that God’s attributes are eternal, so they never had a state of non-permanence. Hence they can be permanence by themselves without the same impossibility.
This distinction is crucial for the Maturidi defense against attributing temporal features to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لهذا المعنى، ولأنها لو كانت حياة لنفسها لكانت حية، ويستحيل أن تكون قادرة عالمة، والقول بحي يستحيل أن يكون عالما قادرا محال فإن قيل: يلزمكم على هذا الأصل القول بجواز بقاء الأعراض، فيكون كل عرض بقاء لنفسه، فيكون السواد ادا للجسم القائم به بنفسه، بقاء لنفسه قيل لهم: إن العرض لا يجوز أن يكون بقاء لنفسه، لأن كل عرض محدث وفى أول ما دخل في حيز الوجود يستحيل أن يكون باقيا، لأنه عبارة عن الدوام، ولا دوام في تلك الحالة (ونفسه في تلك الحالة) موجودة ولم تكن بقاء لما بينا من الاستحالة، فلم يكن باقيا ببقاء هو نفسه، لأن نفسه ليست ببقاء لأنها في تلك الحالة التي بينا موجودة ولم تكن بقاء إذ لو كانت نفسه (بقاء لنفسه لكانت نفسه) باقية في تلك الحالة لاستحالة حصول بقاء لا باقي به. وهذا بخلاف صفات الله تعالى فإنها لم تكن محدثة ليكون له حالة لم تكن فيها باقية ولا ذاتها كانت ببقاء بل كانت أزلية فلم يوجد فيها دليل استحالة كونها بقاء لأنفسها، وقد قام دليل كونها بقاء. وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن البقاء الشاهد ينبغي أن يكون جائز البقاء، لأنه في أول ما دخل في حيز الوجود بقاء فإنا نقول: نعم هو في تلك الحالة ولكن للذات الذي كان بقاء له فأما لنفسه فليس ببقاء للحال، لاستحالة كونه باقيا في تلك الحالة. والله الموفق. ويثبت بهذا أنا لم نقل بباق بلا بقاء ولا بقيام صفة بصفة.
For this meaning, and because if they (accidents) were life by themselves they would be living, and it is impossible for them to be powerful or knowing, and saying of a living thing that it is impossible to be knowing and powerful is absurd. If it is said: You are forced by this principle to say that the permanence of accidents is permissible, so every accident would be permanence by itself, so blackness would be an 'ada' for the body subsisting by itself, permanence by itself. It is said to them: An accident cannot be permanence by itself, because every accident is originated, and in the first moment it enters into existence it is impossible for it to be permanent, because permanence is expression of duration, and there is no duration in that state (and itself in that state) exists and was not permanence due to what we explained of impossibility. Thus it was not permanent by a permanence that is itself, because itself is not permanence, because it in that state which we explained exists and was not permanence, since if itself were (permanence by itself, itself would be) permanent in that state due to the impossibility of obtaining a permanence without something permanent by it. This is contrary to the attributes of God Almighty, for they were not originated such that there would be a state in which they were not permanent, nor was their essence by permanence, but they were eternal, so there is no evidence of impossibility of their being permanence by themselves, and evidence has been established that they are permanence. And by this they are answered concerning their statement: 'The observed permanence should be permissible in permanence, because in the first moment it entered into existence it is permanence.' So we say: Yes, it is in that state, but for the essence which is permanent by it; as for itself, it is not permanence in the case, due to the impossibility of its being permanent in that state. And God is the Grantor of success. And by this it is established that we do not say 'permanent without permanence' nor 'subsistence of an attribute by an attribute'.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that if the author's principle is accepted, then accidents too would have to persist by themselves, making each accident its own permanence. The author responds by distinguishing between originated accidents and eternal divine attributes. An accident, being originated (muḥdath), cannot be permanent in its first moment because permanence (baqā') implies duration (dawām), and in the first moment of existence there is no duration. Therefore, an accident cannot be permanent by a permanence that is itself. This does not apply to God's attributes, which are eternal (azaliyya) and never had a state of non-permanence, so there is no impossibility in their being permanence by themselves. The author then addresses a further objection about observed permanence, clarifying that such permanence belongs to the essence that is permanent, not to itself. The conclusion affirms that this position avoids saying 'permanent without permanence' or 'attribute subsisting by attribute'.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqā')
عرض (ʿaraḍ)
محدث (muḥdath)
أزلي (azalī)
دوام (dawām)
صفات الله (ṣifāt Allāh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If accidents cannot persist by themselves, then divine attributes cannot either.
Correction: Divine attributes are eternal and never experienced a state of non-permanence, unlike originated accidents, so the same impossibility does not apply.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why cannot an accident be permanent by itself in its first moment?
A) Because it is eternal.
B) Because it is originated and has no duration in its first moment.
C) Because it is a divine attribute.
D) Because it is blackness.

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot persist by themselves because they are originated and lack duration at inception, unlike God's eternal attributes which never had a state of non-permanence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even a single accident like blackness cannot persist by itself for a single moment, how can you depend on your own actions, worship, or good deeds to last? In what one area of your life are you acting as if your efforts can stand without God's continuous, eternal support? What will you change today to rely fully on His attribute of permanence rather than your transient works?
?
The author distinguishes between accidents that cannot be self-permanent and God's attributes that are eternal by themselves. Is there a created attribute or quality in yourself—intelligence, patience, righteousness—that you secretly treat as self-sufficient and lasting? How might this lesson expose that hidden assumption, and what would it look like to repent from that subtle form of shirk?
?
This passage forces you to admit that nothing in creation can sustain its own existence. Which area of your life are you most tempted to feel self-sufficient? Before you finish this reflection, write down one concrete step you will take this week to consciously attribute your continued existence and goodness to God's eternal permanence, not your own.

Knowledge as Permanence vs. Identity with Essence
The author responds to an objection by distinguishing his view on knowledge as permanence from Abu al-Hudhayl's identification of knowledge with essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the Ash'ari position that God's knowledge is a permanent attribute and Abu al-Hudhayl's view that knowledge is identical to God's essence.

Core Idea
Knowledge is permanence, not identity with essence.

This passage addresses an objection against the Ash'ari claim that God's knowledge is permanent by itself. The author distinguishes his view from the Mu'tazili Abu al-Hudhayl's identification of knowledge with essence, highlighting the logical consequences of each position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the question-and-answer format.
The objection is presented as a hypothetical: 'If they say...'
2
Identify the objection
The objector asks why the author can say knowledge is permanent by itself but Abu al-Hudhayl cannot say knowledge is identical to essence.
The objection equates the two positions superficially.
3
Follow the author's response
The author explains that his position says 'knowledge is permanence' (a property), while Abu al-Hudhayl says 'knowledge is essence' (identity).
Note the distinction between 'by itself' and 'identical to essence'.
4
Understand the absurd consequences
Abu al-Hudhayl's view leads to all attributes collapsing into essence, creating contradictions like 'living by knowledge' or 'powerful by life'.
The author lists these consequences to show impossibility.
5
Contrast with author's position
The author's view avoids these consequences because it only says knowledge is permanence, not that it is power or life.
Thus the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا لما جاز لكم أن تقولوا إن علم الله تعالى باق ببقاء هو نفسه: لم لم يجز لأبي الهذيل أن يقول: - إن الله تعالى عالم بعلم هو نفسه ـ قلنا: إنا لما قلنا: إن بقاء العلم بنفسه قلنا: إن العلم بقاء وهذا جائز وهو يقول: علم الله تعالى ذاته، ولا يقول إن ذاته علم وهذا محال ولو قال ذلك لكان أيضا محالا، ولأنه كما يجعل علمه ذاته كذا يجعل قدرته وسمعه وبصره وبقاءه ذاته، فيجعله حيا بما هو علم، قادرا بما هو حياة، سميعا بما هو حياة، سميعا بما هو قدرة، وهذا كله محال (ونحن إذا قلنا: إن العلم بقاء لم نقل إنه قدرة أو سمع أو بصر، وكان ما قاله محالا). وما قلنا صحيحا. والله الموفق.
If they say: Since it is permissible for you to say that the knowledge of God is permanent by a permanence that is itself, why is it not permissible for Abu al-Hudhayl to say: God is knowing by a knowledge that is Himself? We say: When we said that the permanence of knowledge is by itself, we say that knowledge is permanence, and this is permissible. But he says that the knowledge of God is His essence, and he does not say that His essence is knowledge, and this is impossible. And if he said that, it would also be impossible. And because just as he makes His knowledge His essence, so he would make His power, hearing, sight, and permanence His essence, thus he would make Him living by what is knowledge, powerful by what is life, hearing by what is life, hearing by what is power — all of this is impossible. (And we, when we say that knowledge is permanence, do not say that it is power or hearing or sight, and what he said is impossible.) And what we said is correct. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection comparing the author's position with that of the Mu'tazili Abu al-Hudhayl. The objector asks why the Ash'ari can say that God's knowledge is permanent by a permanence that is itself, while Abu al-Hudhayl cannot say that God is knowing by a knowledge that is identical to His essence. The author explains: when they say 'knowledge is permanence (baqā')', they mean that knowledge is a property that endures, which is permissible. Abu al-Hudhayl, by contrast, identifies the attribute (knowledge) with the essence (dhāt) itself. This leads to absurd consequences: if knowledge is the essence, then power, hearing, sight, and permanence would also be the essence, resulting in contradictions like 'living by knowledge' or 'powerful by life'. The author's position avoids this because it only states that knowledge is permanence, not that it is power or life. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
علم (ʿilm)
بقاء (baqā')
ذات (dhāt)
أبو الهذيل (Abū al-Hudhayl)
محال (muḥāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'knowledge is permanence' means knowledge is identical to the essence.
Correction: The author shows that 'knowledge is permanence' means it endures, not that it is the essence. Abu al-Hudhayl's error is equating attribute with essence.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between the author's position and Abu al-Hudhayl's regarding knowledge?
A) The author says knowledge is permanent by itself; Abu al-Hudhayl says knowledge is identical to the essence.
B) Both say knowledge is identical to the essence.
C) The author says knowledge is the essence; Abu al-Hudhayl says knowledge is permanent.
D) Neither talks about knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view of knowledge as a permanent attribute avoids absurd consequences because it does not identify knowledge with the essence, unlike Abu al-Hudhayl's position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been tempted to think of God's attributes as mere human-like qualities, forgetting that they are eternal and distinct from His essence? How does this lesson correct that assumption? What will you do to train your heart to think of divine attributes with true reverence?
?
When you pray 'Ya 'Aleem (O All-Knowing),' do you truly reflect that His knowledge is an eternal, real attribute that endures without beginning? Or do you passively recite it? How can this understanding deepen your reliance on His knowledge today?
?
If someone asked you whether God's knowledge is the same as His essence, how would you answer based on this passage? Does your answer affect how you trust in His attributes? What practical difference does it make in your daily life?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila argument on otherness of attributes
Dismisses the Mu'tazila claim as arbitrary and proof-less.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazila claim that attributes are 'other than God' in pre-eternity by dismissing it as arbitrary and lacking proof.

Core Idea
Accusations without proof are empty arbitrariness.

This passage presents a classic example of theological refutation. The Mu'tazila argued that if God has attributes, those attributes would be 'other than God', which is impossible in pre-eternity. The author does not engage in a lengthy counter-argument; instead, he exposes the weakness of the claim by calling it pure arbitrariness and demanding a proof. This approach highlights the importance of evidence in Islamic theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the author's direct and dismissive tone.
The word 'صدة' may be a corruption of 'صفة' (attribute).
2
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
Identify the two components: (1) an attribute would be 'other than God' (غير الله), (2) having something other than God in pre-eternity is impossible.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author calls the argument pure arbitrariness (تحكم محض) and a claim devoid of proof. He then states that it suffices to deny the premise and invalidate their definition of 'otherness'.
This is a standard refutation technique: manʿ (denial of the premise).
4
Reflect on the concluding question
The author ends with 'Why did you say that?'—a rhetorical question that exposes the lack of evidence. Consider why this is an effective response.
The burden of proof lies on the claimant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما شبهتهم أن الله تعالى لو كان له صدة لكانت غير الله، والقول بوجود غير الله تعالى في الأزل محال. فهو تحكم محض ودعوى متجردة عن البرهان، ويكفي المنع وإبطال ما يجعلونه حدا للغيرين جوابا عنه فيقال لهم: لم قلتم ذلك؟
As for their doubt that if God had an attribute it would be other than God, and the statement that the existence of something other than God in pre-eternity is impossible — it is pure arbitrariness and a claim devoid of proof. And it suffices to deny [the premise] and to invalidate what they make a definition for the two 'others' as a response to it. So it is said to them: Why did you say that?
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage refutes a Mu'tazila doubt (shubha). They argue that if God had an attribute (ṣifah), that attribute would be 'other than God' (ghayr Allāh), and the existence of something 'other than God' in pre-eternity (azal) is impossible. The author dismisses this as pure arbitrariness (taḥakkum) and a claim without proof (burhān). He states that it suffices to deny the premise (manʿ) and to invalidate their definition of 'otherness' (ghayriyya). The response is simply to ask them: 'Why did you say that?' — i.e., they have not provided a valid argument for their claim. This refutation does not require a detailed counter-argument; it points out the lack of evidence.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha)
غير (ghayr)
أزل (azal)
تحكم (taḥakkum)
برهان (burhān)
منع (manʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila argument is logically sound because an attribute does seem distinct from the essence.
Correction: The author shows that the argument is arbitrary because the term 'other than God' is not clearly defined, and the claim that an attribute would be a separate entity in pre-eternity is asserted without proof.

Checkpoint
How does the author characterize the Mu'tazila argument that an attribute would be other than God?
A) As a strong proof.
B) As pure arbitrariness and a claim without proof.
C) As a valid objection.
D) As a definition of otherness.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argument about attributes being 'other than God' is dismissed as arbitrary and lacking proof; a simple denial of the premise suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a claim about God without demanding proof? How does this lesson's insistence on proof challenge your own approach to theological beliefs? What is one belief you hold that you have not examined for evidence?
?
The Mu'tazila made an assumption about 'otherness'. Are there assumptions in your own understanding of God that you have never questioned? Will you now investigate their basis?
?
The author says 'it suffices to deny the premise'. When in your life have you needed to simply reject a false premise rather than engage in lengthy debate? How can this strengthen your faith?
