Distinguishing Two Senses of Otherness in Divine Attributes
Objection and answer on attributes being 'others' relative to creation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi response to the accusation that divine attributes imply multiplicity by distinguishing between 'otherness' relative to creation and 'mutual otherness' in themselves.

Core Idea
Being 'other' than creation does not make attributes 'other' from each other.

This passage addresses a second deception used by opponents to confuse the unwary: they argue that since both God's essence and His knowledge are 'other' than humans, they must be 'other' from each other, implying multiplicity. The Maturidi answer carefully distinguishes two senses of 'otherness', using a simple analogy to clarify that being other than a third thing does not require mutual otherness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the objection and answer come through.
2
Identify the objection
Notice how the opponents argue: essence is other than human, knowledge is other than human → therefore essence and knowledge are two 'others' → implying multiplicity in God. Identify the logical step where they go wrong.
The deception lies in moving from 'other relative to creation' to 'mutually other'.
3
Understand the answer and analogy
The answer concedes the first type of otherness (relative to creation) but denies the second (mutual otherness). The analogy of two blacknesses each being different from whiteness but not from each other illustrates the point. Grasp why the analogy works.
The analogy shows that two things can share a relationship to a third without being related in the same way to each other.
4
Apply the principle
Reflect: How does this distinction protect the unity of God's attributes? Write a brief note explaining why the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهم تمويه آخر يلبسون به على الضعفة فيقولون : إن ذات الله غير الإنسان وكذا علمه غير الإنسان، فكان ذاته غير الإنسان، وكذا علمه، فكان كل واحد منهما غيرا فيثنى فيقال: هما غيران فكذا في سائر الصفات. والجواب: أنا لا ننكر كون الذات والصفات أغيارا للمحدثات، وإنما أنكرنا أن تكون متغايرة في أنفسها. وهذا كما يقال في السوادين أن كل واحد منهما مخالف للبياض، ولا يجب أن يكونا مخالفين لأنفسهما، فيبطل هذا التمويه.
And they have another deception by which they confuse the weak: they say that God's essence is other than human, and likewise His knowledge is other than human. So His essence is other than human, and likewise His knowledge, so each one of them is an 'other', so the dual is used and it is said: they are two 'others'. Likewise for the rest of the attributes. The answer: we do not deny that the essence and attributes are 'others' relative to originated things; rather, we deny that they are mutually other in themselves. This is like how it is said regarding two blacknesses that each one of them is different from whiteness, but they are not necessarily different from each other. So this deception is invalidated.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its refutation. The objectors use a deception (tamwih) to confuse the weak: they argue that God's essence is 'other' (ghayr) than human, and His knowledge is also 'other' than human. So each of them (essence and knowledge) is an 'other' relative to humans, and thus they are two 'others' (ghayran) when compared to humans. From this, they conclude that the attributes are 'others' in themselves, implying multiplicity. The answer clarifies that we do not deny that essence and attributes are 'others' relative to created things (muhdathat). What we deny is that they are mutually other (mutaghayira) in themselves (fi anfusihima). The author gives an analogy: two blacknesses are each different from whiteness, but they are not necessarily different from each other. Similarly, essence and knowledge are both 'other' than creation, but this does not necessitate that they are 'other' from each other. Thus the deception fails.

Key Terms
تمويه (deception)
ضعفة (the weak)
غير (other)
محدثات (originated things)
متغايرة (mutually other)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are both 'other' than a third thing, they must be 'other' from each other.
Correction: The analogy of two blacknesses shows that two things can each be different from a third without being different from each other. Similarly, essence and knowledge are both 'other' than creation but not 'other' from each other.

Checkpoint
What point does the analogy of two blacknesses (sawadayn) being different from whiteness but not from each other illustrate?
A) That attributes are multiple in the same way as colors
B) That being 'other' relative to something else does not imply mutual otherness
C) That whiteness is a better analogy for divine attributes
D) That blacknesses are not actually different from each other

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi response preserves divine unity by affirming that attributes are 'other' than creation but not 'other' from each other in themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes like His knowledge and power, do you unconsciously imagine them as separate 'things' inside God? How does this lesson's distinction between being 'other than creation' and being 'other from each other' challenge your mental picture? Will you now actively resist picturing attributes as separate entities?
?
If the attributes are not 'other' from each other in themselves, then every attribute you know (e.g., the All-Knowing, the All-Powerful) refers to one undivided reality. Does your heart truly worship one God when you recite His names, or do you unintentionally fragment Him in your mind? What will you do today to cultivate a more unified awareness of God in your prayer and daily life?

Objection to Proving God's Knowledge from the Witnessed
Objection and partial answer on proof of God's knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the proof for knowledge in the witnessed world relies on ignorance, and why this analogy fails for God, as well as the beginning of the response from the Maturidi perspective.

Core Idea
Human knowledge from ignorance does not prove God's knowledge.

Theologians debated how to prove God's attributes. One argument from the witnessed world claimed that knowledge is proven by the fact that a knower was once ignorant. Critics objected that this does not apply to God, who was never ignorant. This lesson examines this objection and the start of the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objectors argue that the proof for knowledge in the witnessed is based on the knower being ignorant at some point. This characteristic is absent in God.
2
Note the answer's beginning
The response denies that the proof is based on ignorance, stating it is not the method used to establish knowledge.
3
Reflect on the significance
This shows that different methods are used for proving divine attributes, and that the analogy from the witnessed is not valid for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : إن دليل ثبوت العلم في الشاهد كونه جاهلا مرة، وذا غير موجود في الغائب. الجواب عنه : أن كونه مرة جاهلا ليس بطريق إثبات العلم عندنا بل هو
And their saying: The proof for the existence of knowledge in the witnessed [world] is that it is sometimes ignorant, and this is not present in the unseen [God]. The answer to it: that its being sometimes ignorant is not a means for establishing knowledge according to us, but rather it is
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the beginning of its answer. The objectors argue: The proof for affirming knowledge in the witnessed world (shahid) is that the knower is sometimes ignorant (implying that knowledge arises from ignorance), but this characteristic (being previously ignorant) is not present in God (the unseen, gha'ib). Therefore, the analogy fails, and God's knowledge cannot be proven by this method. The answer begins by stating that according to us (the Ash'ari/Maturidi school), the fact that someone is sometimes ignorant is not the means (tariq) by which we establish knowledge. The answer cuts off, but the implication is that our proof for God's knowledge is different, likely based on the contingency of the world or the need for a perfect creator. The passage is incomplete, but the objection and the initial response are clear.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
جاهل
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that proving God's knowledge requires an analogy from human knowledge based on ignorance.
Correction: The Maturidi response shows that this is not the accepted method; the proof for God's knowledge does not rely on the presence of ignorance.

Checkpoint
What do the objectors claim about the proof of knowledge in the witnessed?
A) It is based on the knower being knowledgeable
B) It is based on the knower being sometimes ignorant
C) It is based on the knower being eternal
D) It is based on the knower being a substance

Key Takeaway
This lesson shows that the objection against proving God's knowledge from the witnessed world relies on a flawed analogy, and the Maturidi response denies that ignorance is the basis for establishing knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an unexpected trial, do your emotions reveal that you think God was caught off guard? This lesson teaches that His knowledge is not like ours. How will you remind yourself today that He knew this moment before it came?
?
The critics used a flawed analogy to limit God's knowledge. Are there areas in your life where you unconsciously think God feels or thinks like you do? What specific belief will you correct today based on this lesson?
?
Human knowledge grows from ignorance, but God's knowledge is perfect from eternity. Does your trust in God's plan reflect this truth? In what situation this week will you act on the certainty that God does not learn about your circumstances but knows fully from eternity?

The Muhaqqiqun's Approach to Linguistic Terms for Attributes
The Muhaqqiqun among the proponents of attributes clarify their position on using the term 'other' for attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the muhaqqiqun avoid affirming or denying the term 'other' for attributes, focusing instead on the inseparable relationship between Essence and attributes.

Core Idea
The debate over 'al-ghayr' is linguistic, not theological—what matters is the inseparability of God's Essence and attributes.

The passage presents the stance of the verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) among those who affirm divine attributes. They refuse to get caught in the linguistic game of whether attributes can be called 'other' (ghayr) than the Essence. Instead, they focus on affirming God's existence and attributes, their eternal inseparability, and the impossibility of a second eternal self-subsisting being. This lesson explains their reasoning and why they consider the linguistic dispute a distraction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the muhaqqiqun start by stating what they do not do.
2
Identify the core theological affirmation
The muhaqqiqun affirm God's existence and attributes, asserting that non-existence is impossible for both. They also affirm the inseparability of Essence and attributes.
3
Understand their non-engagement with 'al-ghayr'
They refuse to engage with the term 'other' by negation or affirmation. They consider the linguistic debate irrelevant as long as the inseparability is maintained.
This is a key point: the dispute is reduced to a linguistic issue.
4
Grasp the strategic reason
The muhaqqiqun shift the focus from terminology to the real need: affirming God as eternal with attributes and rejecting any other self-subsisting eternal.
This shows that the Mu'tazila's objection is considered misplaced.
5
Reflect on the lesson's takeaway
Think about how this position avoids theological pitfalls while respecting the language of the Qur'an and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن محققي أصحاب الصفات من يقول : أنا لا أتعرض للفظة الغير بالنفي ولا بالإثبات بل أقول : الله تعالى موجود وله صفات يستحيل عليها العدم كما يستحيل على الذات، وأثبت هذه الصفات بالدليل على حسب ما أثبت الذات، وأقول: لا يتصور بقاء الذات مع عدم الصفات ولا بقاء الصفات مع الذات، ولا حاجة إلى التسمية بالغير وعدم التسمية فإن كان في مثل هذا يطلق اسم الغير لغة لا أبالي بعد أن لا أجوز بقاء الذات مع عدمها، ولا بقاءها مع عدم الذات، لا أجوز غير الذات قائما بذات موصوفا بصفات الكمال بل أقول ذلك مستحيل، وإن كان لا يطلق اسم الغير على ذلك فلا منفعة لي فيه أيضا وإلا فالاشتغال بهذا الكلام من إفلاس المعتزلة لما أن حقيقته ترجع إلى الاختلاف أن اسم الغير في اللغة ينطلق على ماذا، ولا حاجة بنا إلى معرفة حقيقة هذا الاسم في اللغة، بل الحاجة إلى إثبات قديم له صفات الكمال، ونفي ما وراء قديم واحد إذا كان ذلك الثاني قائما بالذات موصوفا بصفات الكمال لما عرفنا بطلان ذلك وامتناعه، وما نحن فيه ليس من هذا القبيل. والله الموفق.
And among the verifying scholars of the proponents of attributes is one who says: I do not engage with the term 'the other' (al-ghayr) by negation or affirmation, but I say: Allah Most High is existent and He has attributes upon which non-existence is impossible just as it is impossible upon the Essence (al-dhat). And I affirm these attributes by proof in the same way I affirmed the Essence. And I say: it is inconceivable that the Essence remain while attributes are absent, nor that attributes remain while the Essence is absent. And there is no need to name as 'other' or not to name. If in such a case the name 'other' is linguistically applied, I do not care after I do not permit the subsistence of the Essence without them, nor their subsistence without the Essence. I do not permit an other of the Essence subsisting by itself qualified by attributes of perfection; rather I say that is impossible. And if the name 'other' is not applied to that, there is also no benefit for me in it. Otherwise, occupying oneself with this speech is from the bankruptcy of the Mu'tazila, because its reality reduces to disagreement over what the name 'other' refers to in language. And we have no need to know the reality of this name in language; rather the need is to affirm an eternal (qadim) having attributes of perfection, and to negate anything beyond a single eternal if that second is subsisting by itself qualified by attributes of perfection, since we have known its falseness and impossibility. And what we are in is not of this kind. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents the position of the verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) among the proponents of divine attributes (ashab al-sifat) regarding the use of the term 'al-ghayr' (the other) for the attributes. The author begins by stating that these scholars refrain from either denying or affirming the term 'other' for the attributes. Instead, they affirm God's existence and His attributes, asserting that non-existence is impossible for both the Essence and the attributes. They affirm the attributes through proof just as they affirm the Essence. They then argue that the Essence cannot be conceived without the attributes, nor the attributes without the Essence. They consider the debate over the term 'other' as linguistically irrelevant: if the language permits calling the attributes 'other', they do not object as long as they maintain the inseparability of Essence and attributes; if it does not permit, they see no benefit in insisting. The core of the matter, they say, is not linguistic but theological: to affirm God as eternal with attributes of perfection, and to reject any second eternal that is self-subsisting and qualified with perfection, which they know to be false. They conclude that their own position falls outside that problematic category.

Key Terms
الغير
الصفات
الذات
قديم
صفات الكمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) To determine whether the attributes can be called 'other' in Arabic.
B) To affirm God as eternal with attributes of perfection and negate any other self-subsisting eternal.
C) To prove that the attributes are identical with the Essence.
D) To avoid using any term for the attributes.

Key Takeaway
The muhaqqiqun teach us that theological disputes should focus on essential truths, not linguistic technicalities.

The Purpose of the Argument from Ignorance
Nasafi clarifies the Ahl al-Sunna's use of the ignorance argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Ahl al-Sunna use the fact that the knower was once ignorant not to prove knowledge, but to prove the distinction between knowledge and the Essence.

Core Idea
The argument from ignorance proves distinction, not knowledge itself.

Imam al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila's objection by clarifying the actual function of the argument that the knower was once ignorant. He explains that this argument is used by the Ahl al-Sunna to demonstrate that knowledge is a separate attribute from the Essence, not that God acquired knowledge over time. This lesson focuses on that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the Mu'tazila's objection and Nasafi's initial response.
2
Identify the objection and response
Find the Mu'tazila's claim and note how Nasafi begins his answer. He says the argument is 'not a way of proving knowledge' but a way of proving 'distinction'.
Notice the key term 'mughayara'.
3
Understand the Ahl al-Sunna's use
Reflect on why the Ahl al-Sunna do not use this as a proof for knowledge. The passage says it proves that knowledge is distinct from the essence because the essence can exist without knowledge.
This is a methodological point, not a substantive proof of God's knowledge.
4
Recognize the inference to a meaning beyond
Nasafi states that from the distinction, one infers a 'meaning beyond the Essence'. This is the foundation for affirming attributes as real and distinct.
This connects to the broader Ash'ari and Maturidi doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : إن دليل ثبوت العلم في الشاهد كونه جاهلا مرة، وذا غير موجود في الغائب. الجواب عنه : أن كونه مرة جاهلا ليس بطريق إثبات العلم عندنا بل هو طريق إثبات المغايرة بين العلم والذات في الشاهد حيث أثبتنا وجود الذات عدم العلم، غير أنا اشتغلنا به وذكرناه ليكون ألزم عليهم، لأن المغايرة بين الشيء وذاته لن تتصور، فيستدل بثبوت المغايرة على ثبوت معنى وراء الذات ثم يقال له: هب أن هذا عندك دليل ثبوت العلم، ولكن لم قلت: إن نقل الدليل شرط لثبوت المدلول ؟ ولم قلت : إن الدليل إذا وجد في محل وظهر أن هذا النوع من المعنى معنى وراء الذات يشترط بعد ذلك نقل الدليل لثبوت ذلك المعنى إلى كل محل؟ أليس أن سواد الغراب والفأر وبياض الثلج والكافور معاني وراء الذات، وإن لم يوجد نقل الدليل لعدم رؤيتنا هذه الأشياء متعرية عن هذه الألوان فبطل ما قالوه، مع أن فيما ذكرنا قبل هذا من دلالة الفعل المحكم على العلم ما يفنينا عن ابتداء جواب لهذا النوع من الكلام. وبالله العصمة والتوفيق.
And their statement: 'The proof of the existence of knowledge in the witnessed is that it was once ignorant, and that does not exist in the unseen.' The answer to it: That it was once ignorant is not, according to us, a way of proving knowledge; rather it is a way of proving the distinction (al-mughayara) between knowledge and the Essence in the witnessed, where we have established the existence of the Essence without knowledge. However, we engaged with it and mentioned it to be more binding upon them, because distinction between a thing and itself cannot be conceived. So one infers from the establishment of distinction the establishment of a meaning (ma'na) beyond the Essence. Then it is said to him: 'Grant that according to you this is a proof of the existence of knowledge, but why did you say that transferring the proof is a condition for the establishment of the proved? And why did you say that when a proof is found in a locus and it becomes apparent that this type of meaning is a meaning beyond the Essence, then transferring the proof is required to establish that meaning in every locus? Is not the blackness of the crow and the mouse and the whiteness of snow and camphor meanings beyond the Essence, even though a transferred proof does not exist because we have not seen these things stripped of these colors?' Thus what they said is falsified, especially since what we mentioned before this concerning the indication of the well-ordered act (fi'l muhkam) upon knowledge suffices us from needing to answer this type of speech at all. And with Allah is protection and success.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage records an objection by the Mu'tazila and its refutation. The Mu'tazila claim that the proof for the existence of knowledge in the witnessed world (al-shahid) is that the knower was once ignorant, and this proof (being ignoran) does not exist in the unseen (al-gha'ib), implying that knowledge cannot be proven for God. The author responds by clarifying that for the Ahl al-Sunna, the fact that the knower was once ignorant is not a proof for the existence of knowledge; rather it is a proof for the distinction (mughayara) between knowledge and the Essence. They use it to show that knowledge is a separate meaning (ma'na) beyond the Essence, because if it were identical, the Essence could not be sometimes knowing and sometimes ignorant. They then challenge the Mu'tazila: even if we accept this as a proof for knowledge, why do they insist that the same proof must be transferable to every locus? The author gives examples: blackness in a crow and a mouse are both black, but we have not seen them stripped of blackness; yet we know blackness is a meaning beyond their essences. Similarly, the proof of distinction does not need to be repeated in the unseen to establish that attributes are meanings beyond the Essence. Finally, the author notes that they have already given a more sufficient proof from the well-ordered act of creation, making this response unnecessary.

Key Terms
دليل ثبوت العلم
الشاهد
الغائب
المغايرة
المعنى وراء الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that the argument from ignorance is the direct proof for knowledge in the witnessed and that the same proof must be transferable to the unseen.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna use the argument to prove distinction, not as a direct proof for knowledge, and they do not require the same proof to be transferable.

Checkpoint
What is the actual function of the argument from ignorance in Ahl al-Sunna theology?
A) It proves that God has knowledge.
B) It proves the distinction between knowledge and the Essence.
C) It proves that God was once ignorant.
D) It proves that knowledge is identical with the Essence.

Key Takeaway
The argument that the knower was once ignorant is not used by Ahl al-Sunna to prove the existence of knowledge in God, but to prove that knowledge is a distinct attribute from the Essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your own knowledge and ignorance as distinct from your self? When you learn something new, do you feel your essence changes? How does this lesson affect how you understand God's perfection?
?
Have you ever rejected an attribute of God because you couldn't find a perfect parallel in your own experience? How does Nasafi's correction of the Mu'tazila challenge you to think more carefully about divine attributes?
?
Nasafi mentions that Ahl al-Sunna engage with the ignorance argument 'to be more binding upon them.' When you discuss your faith, do you ever argue on your opponent's terms even when those terms misrepresent your own beliefs? What would it cost you to clarify your actual position first?

The Fallacy of Requiring Transferred Proofs
Nasafi refutes the requirement of proof transferability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's condition that the same witness-based proof must be transferable to the unseen is logically flawed.

Core Idea
A proof's presence in one locus does not require its repetition in every locus.

Imam al-Nasafi continues his refutation by challenging the Mu'tazila's hidden assumption: that a proof used in the witnessed world must be directly transferable to the unseen. He uses the examples of blackness in crows and mice, and whiteness in snow and camphor, showing that we can know these qualities are distinct from their essences without having seen them stripped of those attributes. This lesson unpacks this powerful logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the second part of the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the rhetorical question and the examples.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's condition
Nasafi asks: 'Why did you say that transferring the proof is a condition for the establishment of the proved?' This is the crux of the objection.
The Mu'tazila assume that the proof must be repeated in every locus.
3
Examine the counterexamples
Nasafi gives examples: blackness of the crow and the mouse, whiteness of snow and camphor. We have never seen these objects stripped of their colors, yet we know the colors are meanings beyond their essences.
This shows that a transferred proof is not necessary.
4
Conclude the refutation
Nasafi's examples demonstrate that the Mu'tazila's condition is invalid. The fact that the same proof does not appear in the unseen does not prevent affirming attributes as distinct meanings.
Nasafi also mentions a prior proof from the well-ordered act suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refuting the Mu'tazila Objection: Need Only Between Distinct Entities
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila objection by defining need and applying it to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila argument that God's knowledge implies need fails because need only applies between distinct entities, and God's attributes are inseparable from His essence.

Core Idea
Need only exists between two distinct things, not within the same essence.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God is knowing by knowledge, He would be in need of that knowledge, implying deficiency. This lesson examines their objection and Imam al-Nasafi's response: need requires two distinct entities, but God's essence was never devoid of knowledge, so the concept of need does not apply.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the Mu'tazila claim and the author's immediate reply.
The author begins by stating the objection before giving the true answer.
2
Understand the definition of need
The author defines need as a deficiency that exists between two distinct things. Consider why this definition is crucial.
3
Apply the definition to God
Since God's essence was never devoid of knowledge, there is no lack or need. See why the Mu'tazila analogy fails.
Notice the author's use of 'mutaghayiriyan'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض للمعتزلة والرد عليه وقولهم : لو كان الله تعالى عالما بعلم لكان محتاجا إلى العلم، لأنهم يعارضون بالذات ثم حقيقة الجواب: أن الحاجة لا تكون إلا بين المتغايرين، وكذا الحاجة نقص يرتفع بالمطلوب فتتحقق هي ثم ترتفع بوجود ما به دفعها، ولم تكن الذات متعريا عن العلم، ولم يكن العلم معدوما ليتصور الحاجة واندفاعها. ثم العجب من قوم يجعلون الأصلح للعباد واجبا على الله تعالى إيجابا لو امتنع عن إيجاده ذلك لزالت ربوبيته، ولم يجعلوه محتاجا إلى الإيجاد لإبقاء ربوبيته، وكذا يجعلونه متكلما بكلام أحدثه ومريدا بإرادة حادثة، ولا يجعلونه محتاجا إلى الكلام والإرادة مع كونهما محدثين، ثم يلزمون خصوصهم بإثباتهم الصفات إثبات الحاجة، وهذا هو غاية الوقاحة - عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك.
An objection of the Mu'tazila and the response to it: And their statement: 'If Allah Most High were knowing by knowledge, He would be in need of knowledge.' Because they analogize to the Essence. Then the true answer: That need only exists between two distinct things (mutaghayiriyan). Likewise, need is a deficiency that is removed by the sought thing; so it [the need] is realized then removed by the existence of that which repels it. And the Essence was never devoid of knowledge, nor was knowledge non-existent such that need and its removal could be conceived. Then it is astonishing that a people who make the best (al-aslah) for servants obligatory upon Allah Most High — if He refrained from creating it, His lordship would cease — yet they do not consider Him in need of creating to preserve His lordship. Similarly, they make Him a speaker by a speech He originated and a willer by a will that is originated, yet they do not consider Him in need of speech and will while they are originated. Then they charge their opponents with affirming attributes as affirming need. This is the utmost impudence — may Allah protect us from that.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage presents another objection by the Mu'tazila and its refutation. The Mu'tazila argue that if God is knowing by a knowledge, He would be in need of that knowledge, drawing an analogy with created beings. The author gives the true answer: need only exists between two distinct entities (mutaghayiriyan). Moreover, need is a deficiency that arises from lacking something and is removed when that thing is obtained. But God's Essence was never devoid of knowledge, nor was knowledge non-existent, so the very concept of need does not apply. The author then points out an inconsistency in the Mu'tazila's own positions: they make it obligatory upon God to create what is best for servants, and if He did not, His lordship would cease — yet they do not consider Him in need of creating to preserve His lordship. Similarly, they affirm that God speaks by an originated speech and wills by an originated will, but they do not consider Him in need of these. Yet they charge their opponents (the Ahl al-Sunna) that affirming attributes implies need. The author calls this utter impudence.

المتغايران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That affirming a real attribute of knowledge implies God needs that attribute.
Correction: Need requires two distinct entities, but God's knowledge is not distinct from His essence; it is inherent and eternal.

Checkpoint

A) Because God's knowledge is created.
B) Because need requires two distinct entities and God's essence was never without knowledge.
C) Because knowledge is identical to God's essence.
D) Because need is only physical.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are inseparable from His essence, so the concept of need—which presupposes two distinct things—does not apply.

How does this passage's definition of need—as a deficiency between distinct things—challenge that mindset? The Mu'tazila Inconsistency: Obligating al-Aslah and Originated Attributes
Nasafi points out the Mu'tazila's own inconsistency in their doctrines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position: they obligate al-aslah on God without considering need, and affirm originated attributes but deny need, while accusing opponents of implying need.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila inconsistently deny need for their own doctrines but charge others with it.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a powerful rebuttal: the Mu'tazila themselves hold positions that would imply need if their logic were applied. They make it obligatory for God to create what is best for servants, and they affirm that God speaks by an originated speech and wills by an originated will—yet they do not consider Him in need of these. Then they accuse the Ahl al-Sunna of implying need by affirming attributes.

Guided Path

Analyze the first example
The Mu'tazila make al-aslah obligatory upon God, saying if He didn't create it, His lordship would cease. But they don't consider Him in need of creating it. Ask: why is this inconsistent?
The author points out that this obligation implies a need to preserve lordship, yet they deny need.
3
Analyze the second example
They affirm that God speaks by an originated speech and wills by an originated will, but they don't consider Him in need of these. Yet these originated attributes would be distinct from Him, potentially implying need.
4
Recognize the charge of impudence
The author concludes that the Mu'tazila's accusation against Ahl al-Sunna is the height of impudence, given their own inconsistent positions.

Refuting the Need Argument from Divine Knowledge
Refutes the Mu'tazila objection that divine knowledge entails need (ḥāja).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila claim that divine knowledge implies need (ḥāja) is invalid, based on the definition of need and the inseparable relationship between God's essence and attributes.

Core Idea
Need requires two distinct things; God and His knowledge are not distinct.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God knows by an attribute of knowledge, He must be in need of that knowledge. This lesson presents the Maturidi refutation, which hinges on the precise definition of need (ḥāja). Need only occurs between two separate entities, but God's essence is never devoid of knowledge, so no need exists. The passage also highlights the irony in the Mu'tazila's own positions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Read the opening statement: the Mu'tazila claim that if God is knowing by a knowledge, He would be in need of that knowledge. Why would they think that?
They assume that having a separate attribute implies a need for it.
2
Analyze the refutation's key definition
The refutation defines 'need' (ḥāja) as occurring only between two distinct things (mutaghāyirayn). Since God's essence and knowledge are not distinct in that way, need is impossible.
Also, need involves a deficiency that is removed, but the essence is never without knowledge.
3
Notice the logical inconsistency of the Mu'tazila
The passage points out that the Mu'tazila themselves obligate God to create what is best for servants (though that would imply a need), and they affirm God speaks with an originated speech and wills with an originated will—yet they do not call those 'needs'.
This shows their argument is inconsistent.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author calls the Mu'tazila position 'utmost impudence' for accusing the Ahl al-Sunna of attributing need to God while ignoring their own inconsistencies.
The core insight: attributes do not imply need when they are inseparable from the essence.

Objection of the Mu'tazila and the refutation of it: And their saying: If God Most High were knowing by a knowledge, He would be in need of knowledge, because they argue by the essence. Then the true answer is: Need occurs only between two distinct things. Similarly, need is a deficiency that is removed by the desired thing; so it is realized and then removed by the existence of that which repels it. Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazila and its refutation. The Mu'tazila claim that if God is knowing by a knowledge, He would be in need of that knowledge. The refutation begins by defining 'need' (ḥāja): need only exists between two distinct entities (mutaghāyirayn). Since God's essence and His knowledge are not distinct in the sense of one being separate from the other (the essence is never devoid of knowledge), there is no genuine need. Furthermore, need implies a deficiency that is removed by obtaining what is needed, but neither the essence nor knowledge is absent. The author then points out the hypocrisy of the Mu'tazila: they obligate God to create what is best for servants, threatening that He would lose lordship otherwise, yet they do not call that 'need'. Similarly, they affirm that God speaks with an originated speech and wills with an originated will, yet they do not call that 'need'. Yet they accuse the Ahl al-Sunna of attributing need to God by affirming eternal attributes. The author calls this the utmost impudence.

The Mu'tazila Objection on God's Knowledge
Presents the Mu'tazila's objection and Abu l-Hudhayl's argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's objection that God's knowledge of His knowledge leads to either ignorance or infinite regress, and the argument of Abu l-Hudhayl.

Core Idea
If God knows His knowledge, infinite regress follows; if not, He is ignorant.

The Mu'tazila raised a sophisticated objection about divine knowledge: if God knows His knowledge, then either He knows it by another knowledge (leading to infinite regress) or by His essence (implying He is knowing by essence alone). Abu l-Hudhayl added a twist: if knowledge can be known by itself, why can't the knower be by Himself? Let's read the objection carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three logical options
The objection presents three possibilities: God does not know His knowledge (ignorance), He knows it by another knowledge (regress), or He knows it by His essence (knowledge by essence). Find each one in the text.
3
Understand Abu l-Hudhayl's analogy
Abu l-Hudhayl argues that if knowledge can be known by itself, then a knower (God) can be by Himself without an attribute. See how this analogy is meant to force a conclusion.
Notice the parallel: 'معلوم بنفسه' and 'عالم بنفسه'.
4
Consider the dilemma
Ask yourself: what is the Mu'tazila trying to prove with this objection? They aim to show that God must be knowing by essence, not by an attribute.
This connects to the broader debate on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض آخر والرد عليه
وما يقولون : إن الله تعالى لو كان له علم إن لم يعلم علمه فهو جاهل، وإن كان يعلمه إن كان يعلمه بعلم آخر، فكذلك الكلام في العلم الثاني، وإن كان يعلمه بذاته فقد ثبت أنه عالم بذاته، وإن علم العلم بنفس العلم فهو جعل المعلوم والعلم واحد ولما جاز وجود معلوم بنفسه لم لا يجوز عالم بنفسه، وكذا لما جاز كون معلوم بعلم هو نفسه، لم لا يجوز عالم بعلم هو نفسه؟ كما قال أبو الهذيل. يجاب عنه فيقال: هذا كلام باطل، لأنا نقول : يعلم علمه بعلم هو نفسه إذ علمه شامل بالمعلومات وعلمه معلوم، نظيره ما مر في أول الكتاب من صحة معرفة النظير بالنظير، ولا استحالة في كون المعلوم معلوما إذ لو علم بعلم آخر لجاز انعدام العلم الثاني فيكون الرجل عالما ولا يعلم علمه، فيعلم ولا يعلم أنه يعلم وهو محال، وإذا جاز في الشاهد جاز في الغائب، فأما أن يعلم بما ليس بعلم هو ذاته على ما زعم عامة المعتزلة أو يعلم بعلم هو ذاته فيكون علمه ذاته ولا تكون ذاته علما كما قال أبو الهذيل محال . فأما هاهنا فإن العلم لما كان معلوما بنفسه وكان العلم هو المعلوم كان المعلوم علما ، فلم يكن محالا ، وإنما لزمتنا الاستحالة أن لو قلنا: العلم هو المعلوم والمعلوم ليس بعلم، ونحن لم نقل هكذا، فصح ما قلنا، وبطل ما قال أبو الهذيل.
Another objection and its refutation: And what they say: If God Most High had a knowledge, if He does not know His knowledge, He is ignorant; and if He knows it, if He knows it by another knowledge, then the same applies to the second knowledge; and if He knows it by His essence, then it is established that He is knowing by His essence; and if He knows knowledge by the knowledge itself, then He makes the known and the knowledge one. Since it is not possible for a known to exist by itself, why is it not possible for a knower to be by Himself? Similarly, since it is possible for a known to be by a knowledge that is itself, why is it not possible for a knower to be by a knowledge that is Himself? As Abu l-Hudhayl said. It is answered thus: This is false speech, for we say: He knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is Himself, for His knowledge encompasses all known things and His knowledge is known. The analogy is what passed earlier in the book about the validity of knowing the similar by the similar. There is no impossibility in a known being known, because if it were known by another knowledge, the second knowledge could cease to exist, so a man would be knowing but not know his knowledge—he knows but does not know that he knows, which is absurd. And if it is possible in the witnessed, then it is possible in the unseen. So either He knows by what is not a knowledge that is His essence, as the generality of the Mu'tazila claim, or He knows by a knowledge that is His essence, so His knowledge would be His essence and His essence would not be knowledge, as Abu l-Hudhayl said—that is impossible. But here, since knowledge is known by itself and knowledge is the known, the known is knowledge, so it is not impossible. The impossibility would only follow if we said: knowledge is the known and the known is not knowledge, but we did not say that. So what we said is correct, and what Abu l-Hudhayl said is false.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection from the Mu'tazila and its refutation. The Mu'tazila argue: If God has knowledge, either He knows His knowledge or not. If not, He is ignorant; if He knows it, then either He knows it by another knowledge (leading to infinite regress) or by His essence (implying He is knowing by essence without an attribute). They also cite Abu l-Hudhayl's argument that if knowledge can be known by itself, why can't the knower be by Himself? The refutation states that this argument is false. The author explains that God knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is Himself—His knowledge encompasses all known things, and His knowledge itself is known. This is analogous to the principle from the beginning of the book about knowing the similar by the similar (ma'rifat al-naẓīr bi al-naẓīr). There is no impossibility in a known being known, because if it were known by another knowledge, that second knowledge could cease, leading to a contradiction: a knower who does not know that he knows. This is absurd. Hence, knowledge knows itself. The author distinguishes his position from Abu l-Hudhayl's: Abu l-Hudhayl says knowledge is the essence (making the essence knowledge), which is impossible. But the author says knowledge is known by itself, and the known is knowledge, not the essence. So no impossibility follows.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila objection, what happens if God knows His knowledge by another knowledge?
A) He would be ignorant.
B) It leads to infinite regress.
C) He would be knowing by essence.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that God's knowledge of His knowledge must be either ignorance, infinite regress, or knowledge by essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's knowledge, do you unconsciously picture it as similar to your own—a 'thing' God possesses? How does the Mu'tazila objection expose the danger of anthropomorphizing divine attributes, and what will you do to remember that God's knowledge is beyond any created model?
?
The argument forces a choice: either God knows His knowledge by another knowledge (infinite regress) or by His essence (making attributes redundant). Does this dilemma make you uneasy about the coherence of ‘knowledge’ as an attribute of God? In what area of your life do you need to trust that God's perfection is not bound by human logic, and how will you act on that trust this week?

Refuting Infinite Regress: Knowledge Knows Itself
Presents the refutation using the principle of knowing the similar by the similar and distinguishes from Abu l-Hudhayl's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazila objection by asserting that God knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is Himself, using the principle of 'knowing the similar by the similar,' and how he distinguishes this from Abu l-Hudhayl's error.

Core Idea
God's knowledge knows itself without regress; the similar is known by the similar.

Having presented the Mu'tazila's objection, the author now refutes it. He argues that God knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is Himself—not a separate knowledge, so no regress. He invokes a principle from earlier in his book: the validity of knowing a similar thing by a similar. He also shows that the alternative (knowing by another knowledge) leads to absurdity: a knower who does not know that he knows. Finally, he distinguishes his position from that of Abu l-Hudhayl, who mistakenly identified knowledge with the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow.
2
Identify the core principle
The author uses the principle of 'knowing the similar by the similar' (ma'rifat al-naẓīr bi al-naẓīr). Find it in the text and understand how it applies.
This principle was established earlier in the book.
3
Understand the absurdity argument
The author shows that if knowledge were known by another knowledge, that second knowledge could cease, leading to a knower who does not know his own knowledge—an absurdity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Compare with Abu l-Hudhayl's position
The author contrasts his view with two extremes: the Mu'tazila and Abu l-Hudhayl. He clearly states that he does not equate knowledge with essence, so no impossibility follows.
This is a crucial distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض آخر والرد عليه
وما يقولون : إن الله تعالى لو كان له علم إن لم يعلم علمه فهو جاهل، وإن كان يعلمه إن كان يعلمه بعلم آخر، فكذلك الكلام في العلم الثاني، وإن كان يعلمه بذاته فقد ثبت أنه عالم بذاته، وإن علم العلم بنفس العلم فهو جعل المعلوم والعلم واحد ولما جاز وجود معلوم بنفسه لم لا يجوز عالم بنفسه، وكذا لما جاز كون معلوم بعلم هو نفسه، لم لا يجوز عالم بعلم هو نفسه؟ كما قال أبو الهذيل. يجاب عنه فيقال: هذا كلام باطل، لأنا نقول : يعلم علمه بعلم هو نفسه إذ علمه شامل بالمعلومات وعلمه معلوم، نظيره ما مر في أول الكتاب من صحة معرفة النظير بالنظير، ولا استحالة في كون المعلوم معلوما إذ لو علم بعلم آخر لجاز انعدام العلم الثاني فيكون الرجل عالما ولا يعلم علمه، فيعلم ولا يعلم أنه يعلم وهو محال، وإذا جاز في الشاهد جاز في الغائب، فأما أن يعلم بما ليس بعلم هو ذاته على ما زعم عامة المعتزلة أو يعلم بعلم هو ذاته فيكون علمه ذاته ولا تكون ذاته علما كما قال أبو الهذيل محال . فأما هاهنا فإن العلم لما كان معلوما بنفسه وكان العلم هو المعلوم كان المعلوم علما ، فلم يكن محالا ، وإنما لزمتنا الاستحالة أن لو قلنا: العلم هو المعلوم والمعلوم ليس بعلم، ونحن لم نقل هكذا، فصح ما قلنا، وبطل ما قال أبو الهذيل.
Another objection and its refutation: And what they say: If God Most High had a knowledge, if He does not know His knowledge, He is ignorant; and if He knows it, if He knows it by another knowledge, then the same applies to the second knowledge; and if He knows it by His essence, then it is established that He is knowing by His essence; and if He knows knowledge by the knowledge itself, then He makes the known and the knowledge one. Since it is not possible for a known to exist by itself, why is it not possible for a knower to be by Himself? Similarly, since it is possible for a known to be by a knowledge that is itself, why is it not possible for a knower to be by a knowledge that is Himself? As Abu l-Hudhayl said. It is answered thus: This is false speech, for we say: He knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is Himself, for His knowledge encompasses all known things and His knowledge is known. The analogy is what passed earlier in the book about the validity of knowing the similar by the similar. There is no impossibility in a known being known, because if it were known by another knowledge, the second knowledge could cease to exist, so a man would be knowing but not know his knowledge—he knows but does not know that he knows, which is absurd. And if it is possible in the witnessed, then it is possible in the unseen. So either He knows by what is not a knowledge that is His essence, as the generality of the Mu'tazila claim, or He knows by a knowledge that is His essence, so His knowledge would be His essence and His essence would not be knowledge, as Abu l-Hudhayl said—that is impossible. But here, since knowledge is known by itself and knowledge is the known, the known is knowledge, so it is not impossible. The impossibility would only follow if we said: knowledge is the known and the known is not knowledge, but we did not say that. So what we said is correct, and what Abu l-Hudhayl said is false.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection from the Mu'tazila and its refutation. The Mu'tazila argue: If God has knowledge, either He knows His knowledge or not. If not, He is ignorant; if He knows it, then either He knows it by another knowledge (leading to infinite regress) or by His essence (implying He is knowing by essence without an attribute). They also cite Abu l-Hudhayl's argument that if knowledge can be known by itself, why can't the knower be by Himself? The refutation states that this argument is false. The author explains that God knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is Himself—His knowledge encompasses all known things, and His knowledge itself is known. This is analogous to the principle from the beginning of the book about knowing the similar by the similar (ma'rifat al-naẓīr bi al-naẓīr). There is no impossibility in a known being known, because if it were known by another knowledge, that second knowledge could cease, leading to a contradiction: a knower who does not know that he knows. This is absurd. Hence, knowledge knows itself. The author distinguishes his position from Abu l-Hudhayl's: Abu l-Hudhayl says knowledge is the essence (making the essence knowledge), which is impossible. But the author says knowledge is known by itself, and the known is knowledge, not the essence. So no impossibility follows.

معلوم

نظير
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the author is saying that knowledge and the known are identical in every respect, leading to the accusation that he conflates knowledge with the known.
Correction: The author clarifies that knowledge is known by itself, and the known is knowledge, but he does not say that the knower (God) is identical to knowledge. The impossibility arises only if one equates knowledge with the known in a way that 'the known is not knowledge,' which he does not.

Checkpoint
What principle does the author use to support that God knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is Himself?
A) The principle of cause and effect.
B) The principle of knowing the similar by the similar (ma'rifat al-naẓīr bi al-naẓīr).
C) The principle of verification.
D) The principle of non-contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the infinite regress by arguing that God's knowledge knows itself via the principle of similar knowing similar, and distinguishes his view from the erroneous equation of knowledge with essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that if God's knowledge were known by another knowledge, it could cease, leading to 'knowing without knowing you know'. In your own life, is there a truth about God you intellectually affirm but fail to truly realize in your heart? What would it take for you to move from knowing that God knows to truly knowing that He knows you right now?
?
The author distinguishes between knowledge and essence. Do you ever conflate what you know (your knowledge) with who you are (your essence)? When has your knowledge become a source of pride rather than humility before God? Will you today ask Allah to grant you knowledge that leads to action, not arrogance?
?
God's knowledge knows itself without external validation. Do you constantly seek validation from others for what you know to be true in your faith? How can you cultivate the certainty that God's truth is self-evident, even when the world denies it? Will you commit to relying on the Qur'an and Sunnah as your sole measure of truth?

The Objection: Does God's Knowledge of His Knowledge Lead to Regress?
Objection on divine self-knowledge with a four-branch dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed by Abu al-Hudhayl: if God knows His knowledge, it either requires an infinite regress or forces an unacceptable identity between knowledge and its object.

Core Idea
The objection traps divine self-knowledge between infinite regress and self-identity.

Before the Maturidi reply can be understood, we must first grasp the objection it answers. Abu al-Hudhayl presents a logical dilemma: if God knows His knowledge, it either requires an infinite chain of knowledges or forces an unacceptable identity between knowledge and the knower. This lesson presents that objection in its own terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the four branches of the dilemma.
The objection uses conditional sentences: 'if...then...'.
2
Identify the four possibilities
The objection considers four cases: God does not know His knowledge, knows it by another knowledge, knows it by His essence, or knows it by the same knowledge. Trace each.
The fourth case leads to knowledge and known being one.
3
Understand the conclusion
Abu al-Hudhayl argues that either an infinite regress follows (case 2) or an identity that he finds problematic. Why does he think identity is problematic?
He argues that if knowledge and known are one, then the known would be knowledge, which he thinks is impossible.
4
Note the authority cited
The objection ends by attributing this argument to Abu al-Hudhayl, a prominent Mu'tazili theologian.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض آخر والرد عليه
وما يقولون : إن الله تعالى لو كان له علم إن لم يعلم علمه فهو جاهل، وإن كان يعلمه إن كان يعلمه بعلم آخر، فكذلك الكلام في العلم الثاني، وإن كان يعلمه بذاته فقد ثبت أنه عالم بذاته، وإن علم العلم بنفس العلم فهو جعل المعلوم والعلم واحد ولما جاز وجود معلوم بنفسه لم لا يجوز عالم بنفسه، وكذا لما جاز كون معلوم بعلم هو نفسه، لم لا يجوز عالم بعلم هو نفسه؟ كما قال أبو الهذيل. يجاب عنه فيقال: هذا كلام باطل، لأنا نقول : يعلم علمه بعلم هو نفسه إذ علمه شامل بالمعلومات وعلمه معلوم، نظيره ما مر في أول الكتاب من صحة معرفة النظير بالنظير، ولا استحالة في كون المعلوم معلوما إذ لو علم بعلم آخر لجاز انعدام العلم الثاني فيكون الرجل عالما ولا يعلم علمه، فيعلم ولا يعلم أنه يعلم وهو محال، وإذا جاز في الشاهد جاز في الغائب، فأما أن يعلم بما ليس بعلم هو ذاته على ما زعم عامة المعتزلة أو يعلم بعلم هو ذاته فيكون علمه ذاته ولا تكون ذاته علما كما قال أبو الهذيل محال . فأما هاهنا فإن العلم لما كان معلوما بنفسه وكان العلم هو المعلوم كان المعلوم علما ، فلم يكن محالا ، وإنما لزمتنا الاستحالة أن لو قلنا: العلم هو المعلوم والمعلوم ليس بعلم، ونحن لم نقل هكذا، فصح ما قلنا، وبطل ما قال أبو الهذيل.
Another objection and its reply. And they say: If God the Exalted has knowledge, if He does not know His knowledge, then He is ignorant; and if He knows it, if He knows it by another knowledge, then the same discussion applies to the second knowledge; and if He knows it by His essence, then it is established that He is knowing by His essence; and if He knows knowledge by the same knowledge, then that makes the known and the knowledge one. And since it is not possible for a known to exist by itself, why is it not possible for a knower to be by Himself? Likewise, since it is possible for a known to be by a knowledge that is itself, why is it not possible for a knower to be by a knowledge that is Himself? As Abu al-Hudhayl said. The reply is given by saying: This is false speech, because we say: He knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is His essence, since His knowledge encompasses information and His knowledge is known. Its analogy is what has passed at the beginning of the book regarding the validity of knowing the like by the like. And there is no impossibility in the known being known, because if it were known by another knowledge, then the second knowledge could cease to exist, making the man knowledgeable yet not knowing his knowledge, so he would know and not know that he knows, which is impossible. And if it is possible in the witnessed, it is possible in the absent. As for knowing by what is not knowledge, being His essence – according to the claim of the majority of the Mu'tazila – or knowing by knowledge being His essence, so that His knowledge is His essence and His essence is not knowledge, as Abu al-Hudhayl said, it is impossible. But here, since knowledge was known by itself and knowledge is the known, the known became knowledge, so it was not impossible. Only would the impossibility be required if we said: knowledge is the known and the known is not knowledge. And we did not say this. So what we said is correct, and what Abu al-Hudhayl said is false.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the Maturidi view of divine knowledge and the reply. The objection argues that if God knows His own knowledge, it leads to either an infinite regress (if He knows it by another knowledge) or accepting that He knows by His essence or that the knowledge itself knows itself. Abu al-Hudhayl is cited. The reply refutes this by asserting that God knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is His essence, because knowledge is self-referential. The principle of knowing a like by a like is referenced from earlier in the book. It explains that a known can be known by itself without regress. A hypothetical scenario shows that if knowledge were known by a separate knowledge, that second knowledge could vanish, resulting in knowledge without awareness, which is absurd. Since this holds in the witnessed world, it holds for God. The reply distinguishes cases: knowing by what is not knowledge (Mu'tazila) or by knowledge that is essence (Abu al-Hudhayl) are impossible. But here, since knowledge itself is the known, the known becomes knowledge, so no impossibility. The impossibility would arise only if we mistakenly said knowledge and known are different, which we do not.

Checkpoint
In the objection, what is the consequence if God knows His knowledge by another knowledge?
A) He would be ignorant.
B) It leads to an infinite regress.
C) He would know by His essence.
D) Knowledge and known become one.

Key Takeaway
The objection argues that any way God knows His own knowledge leads to an impossibility or infinite regress, challenging the Maturidi view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God knowing every detail of your life, do you ever wonder if He truly 'knows' you, or does that knowledge feel abstract? How does the theological debate about God's self-knowledge challenge you to deepen your relationship with a God whose knowledge is beyond your full comprehension?
?
The objection suggests that if God knows His knowledge by itself, then the known and knowledge become one. How does this idea that God's knowledge is identical with His essence affect your trust in His promises? Are you willing to submit to a God whose very being is knowledge, or do you secretly wish for a God more easy to understand?
?
In moments when you feel distant from God, do you ever doubt that He fully knows your struggles? Reflect on the fact that even the greatest theologians could not fully explain how God knows Himself. Will you choose to trust in His all-encompassing knowledge despite the mystery, or will you let uncertainty shake your faith?

The Maturidi Reply: Knowledge as Self-Referential
Maturidi reply refuting the objection using self-referential knowledge and the principle of like-by-like.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi school refutes the objection by affirming that God knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is His essence, using the principle of knowing the like by the like.

Core Idea
God's knowledge knows itself without regress, just as a like is known by a like.

Having heard the objection, we now turn to the reply. The Maturidi response invokes a principle established early in the book: that a thing can be known by its like. It argues that God's knowledge encompasses all knowns, including itself, without requiring another knowledge. This lesson unpacks the logical force of that reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the reply passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the reply begins by dismissing the objection as false.
2
Find the core affirmation
The reply states: 'He knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is His essence.' Underline this sentence.
This is the key position.
3
Understand the analogy
The reply references an earlier principle: 'knowing the like by the like.' How does this analogy work?
Knowledge is itself a known; it can be known by itself as a like.
4
Examine the argument against regress
The reply gives a hypothetical: if knowledge were known by another knowledge, that second knowledge could vanish, leading to a contradiction. This shows self-referential knowledge is necessary.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Distinguish positions
The reply distinguishes between two impossible positions (Mu'tazila and Abu al-Hudhayl) and its own position, showing why its own is coherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض آخر والرد عليه
وما يقولون : إن الله تعالى لو كان له علم إن لم يعلم علمه فهو جاهل، وإن كان يعلمه إن كان يعلمه بعلم آخر، فكذلك الكلام في العلم الثاني، وإن كان يعلمه بذاته فقد ثبت أنه عالم بذاته، وإن علم العلم بنفس العلم فهو جعل المعلوم والعلم واحد ولما جاز وجود معلوم بنفسه لم لا يجوز عالم بنفسه، وكذا لما جاز كون معلوم بعلم هو نفسه، لم لا يجوز عالم بعلم هو نفسه؟ كما قال أبو الهذيل. يجاب عنه فيقال: هذا كلام باطل، لأنا نقول : يعلم علمه بعلم هو نفسه إذ علمه شامل بالمعلومات وعلمه معلوم، نظيره ما مر في أول الكتاب من صحة معرفة النظير بالنظير، ولا استحالة في كون المعلوم معلوما إذ لو علم بعلم آخر لجاز انعدام العلم الثاني فيكون الرجل عالما ولا يعلم علمه، فيعلم ولا يعلم أنه يعلم وهو محال، وإذا جاز في الشاهد جاز في الغائب، فأما أن يعلم بما ليس بعلم هو ذاته على ما زعم عامة المعتزلة أو يعلم بعلم هو ذاته فيكون علمه ذاته ولا تكون ذاته علما كما قال أبو الهذيل محال . فأما هاهنا فإن العلم لما كان معلوما بنفسه وكان العلم هو المعلوم كان المعلوم علما ، فلم يكن محالا ، وإنما لزمتنا الاستحالة أن لو قلنا: العلم هو المعلوم والمعلوم ليس بعلم، ونحن لم نقل هكذا، فصح ما قلنا، وبطل ما قال أبو الهذيل.
Another objection and its reply. And they say: If God the Exalted has knowledge, if He does not know His knowledge, then He is ignorant; and if He knows it, if He knows it by another knowledge, then the same discussion applies to the second knowledge; and if He knows it by His essence, then it is established that He is knowing by His essence; and if He knows knowledge by the same knowledge, then that makes the known and the knowledge one. And since it is not possible for a known to exist by itself, why is it not possible for a knower to be by Himself? Likewise, since it is possible for a known to be by a knowledge that is itself, why is it not possible for a knower to be by a knowledge that is Himself? As Abu al-Hudhayl said. The reply is given by saying: This is false speech, because we say: He knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is His essence, since His knowledge encompasses information and His knowledge is known. Its analogy is what has passed at the beginning of the book regarding the validity of knowing the like by the like. And there is no impossibility in the known being known, because if it were known by another knowledge, then the second knowledge could cease to exist, making the man knowledgeable yet not knowing his knowledge, so he would know and not know that he knows, which is impossible. And if it is possible in the witnessed, it is possible in the absent. As for knowing by what is not knowledge, being His essence – according to the claim of the majority of the Mu'tazila – or knowing by knowledge being His essence, so that His knowledge is His essence and His essence is not knowledge, as Abu al-Hudhayl said, it is impossible. But here, since knowledge was known by itself and knowledge is the known, the known became knowledge, so it was not impossible. Only would the impossibility be required if we said: knowledge is the known and the known is not knowledge. And we did not say this. So what we said is correct, and what Abu al-Hudhayl said is false.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the Maturidi view of divine knowledge and the reply. The objection argues that if God knows His own knowledge, it leads to either an infinite regress (if He knows it by another knowledge) or accepting that He knows by His essence or that the knowledge itself knows itself. Abu al-Hudhayl is cited. The reply refutes this by asserting that God knows His knowledge by a knowledge that is His essence, because knowledge is self-referential. The principle of knowing a like by a like is referenced from earlier in the book. It explains that a known can be known by itself without regress. Since this holds in the witnessed world, it holds for God. The reply distinguishes cases: knowing by what is not knowledge (Mu'tazila) or by knowledge that is essence (Abu al-Hudhayl) are impossible. But here, since knowledge itself is the known, the known becomes knowledge, so no impossibility. The impossibility would arise only if we mistakenly said knowledge and known are different, which we do not.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge of His knowledge leads to an infinite regress of knowledges.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah knows His own knowledge without any need for a separate attribute, does that make you more certain that He knows every detail of your life? Or do you still live as if some things are hidden from Him? Will you honestly examine what you are keeping from your own awareness today?
?
The passage argues that it is impossible to know without knowing that you know. Are there areas of your faith where you 'know' the truth but refuse to acknowledge your own knowledge? For example, do you know that a particular sin displeases Allah but act as if you don't? What would it cost you to let that knowledge fully penetrate your heart right now?
?

In light of this, how can you align your self-awareness with the truth that Allah sees you completely? What step can you take today to bring a hidden part of yourself into the light of His knowledge?

Abu al-Husayn's Argument and the Distinction of Modes
Presentation of Abu al-Husayn's argument and the initial refutation via mode distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's argument that divine knowledge would be similar to human knowledge if both are by a meaning, and the author's refutation by distinguishing the mode of relation.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is unlike human knowledge because it relates as 'knower' not as 'known'.

Abu al-Husayn al-Basri argued that if God knows by a meaning, that meaning must be similar to the meaning that makes humans knowers, since both relate to the same object. The Maturidi author refutes this by clarifying the mode of relation: human knowledge relates as a relation of the known (t'alluq al-ma'lum), while divine knowledge relates as a relation of the knower (t'alluq al-'alimin). Thus, they are not similar.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where Abu al-Husayn presents his argument that if God knows by a meaning, that meaning would be similar to the meaning by which we know.
Notice he uses the example of Zaid being in the house.
2
Identify the author's distinction
Find where the author distinguishes between 't'alluq al-ma'lum' (relation of the known) and 't'alluq al-'alimin' (relation of the knower).
This is the key refutation.
3
Understand why this refutes the analogy
Reflect on why the difference in mode prevents the two knowledges from being similar (mithl).

وذكر أبو الحسين البصري أن الله تعالى لو كان عالما بمعنى، لكان ذلك المعنى مثلا للمعنى الذي أوجب كوننا عالمين، لأنه متعلق بما تعلق به علمنا على الوجه الذي تعلق به في وقت واحد على طريقة واحدة، ألا ترى أن لنا علما أن زيدا في الدار ، والمعنى الذي يوجب كون الباري سبحانه وتعالى عالما متعلق بزيد هذا بعينه أنه في الدار في هذا الوقت فقد تعلقا لذاتيهما بشيء واحد على وجه واحد في وقت واحد على طريقة واحدة فكانا مثلين وأعني بقولي: على طريقة واحدة أنهما تعلقا به تعلق المعلوم على التفصيل، ولهذا لم يلزم أن يكون علم الواحد منا مثلا للباري سبحانه وتعالى، وإن تعلقا بأن زيدا في الدار لأن العلم منا تعلق بزيد تعلق المعلوم، والباري عز وجل تعلق به تعلق العالمين، فلم يسد أحد الذاتين مسد الآخر. والدلالة على أن كل علمين تعلقا بشيء واحد على الحد الذي ذكرناه فهما مثلان هي أن كل واحد منهما قد سد مسد الآخر، وقام مقامه فيما يرجع إلى ذاته، ألا ترى أنه قد علم بأحدهما ما علم بالآخر على الحد الذي علم بالآخر. وأيضا فلو فرضنا وجود ما يسمونه علم الباري عز وجل بأن زيدا في الدار، ثم طرأ على ذلك المحل اعتقاد بأن زيدا ليس في الدار لنفاهما جميعا لأنه لو نفى أحدهما دون الآخر اجتمع في قلب الإنسان العلم بأن زيدا في الدار، والجهل بأن زيدا في الدار على حد واحد ، وإذا انتفى كلا العلمين بالجهل ثبت أنهما مثلان، لأن الشيء الواحد لا ينفي شيئين مختلفين غير ضدين، ألا ترى أن السواد لا ينفي البياض والحموضة لما كان البياض والحموضة مختلفين غير ضدين، وينفي البياضين عن المحل الواحد لأن البياضين مثلان والبياض والحمرة ضدان.
And Abu al-Husayn al-Basri mentioned that if God the Exalted were knowing by a meaning, that meaning would be similar to the meaning that necessitates our being knowers, because it relates to what our knowledge related to, in the same manner in which it related, at a single time, in a single mode. Do you not see that we have knowledge that Zaid is in the house, and the meaning that necessitates the Creator, glorified and exalted, being a knower relates to this same Zaid, that he is in the house at this time. So they both related, by their essences, to one thing in one manner at one time in one mode, so they would be similar. And by my saying 'in one mode' I mean that they both related to it as a relation of the known to the detailed. And for this reason, it is not necessary that the knowledge of one of us be similar to the knowledge of the Creator, glorified and exalted, even if they both relate that Zaid is in the house, because the knowledge from us relates to Zaid as a relation of the known, while the Creator, mighty and majestic, relates to it as a relation of the knower. So one of the two essences does not take the place of the other. The evidence that every two knowledges that relate to one thing in the limit we mentioned are indeed similar is that each one of them has taken the place of the other, and stood in its position regarding what pertains to its essence. Do you not see that by one of them is known what is known by the other, in the limit in which it is known by the other? Also, if we suppose the existence of what they call the knowledge of the Creator, mighty and majestic, that Zaid is in the house, then a belief arises in that subject that Zaid is not in the house, it would negate both of them. Because if it negated one without the other, then there would gather in the human heart the knowledge that Zaid is in the house and the ignorance that Zaid is in the house in the same degree. And when both knowledges are negated by ignorance, it is established that they are similar, because a single thing does not negate two different things that are not opposites. Do you not see that blackness does not negate whiteness and sourness because whiteness and sourness are different, not opposites, and it negates two whitenesses from a single subject because the two whitenesses are similar, while whiteness and redness are opposites.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage records and refutes an argument from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, a Mu'tazili scholar. He argues that if God knows by a meaning (ma'na), that meaning would be similar (mithl) to the meaning that makes humans knowers because both relate to the same object (Zaid being in the house) in the same manner, at the same time, and in the same mode. The author replies by distinguishing the mode of relation: human knowledge relates as t'alluq al-ma'lum (relation of the known), while divine knowledge relates as t'alluq al-'alimin (relation of the knower). Thus they are not similar. The author then provides a proof: if two knowledges relate to the same thing in the same way, they are substitutes for each other and thus similar. But divine and human knowledge are not substitutes. A second proof: if both supposed divine and human knowledges about Zaid's location were present in a subject, and then a contradictory belief (that Zaid is not in the house) arises, it would negate both, proving they are similar in effect. But the author argues this does not apply because the divine knowledge is not present in the human heart; it is a different mode. The passage concludes by analogy: a single thing can negate two similar things (like two whitenesses) but not two different non-opposites. Thus the argument fails.

Key Terms
معنى
تعلق
علم المعلوم
علم العالمين
مثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing the same object makes two knowledges identical or similar in essence.
Correction: The mode of relation matters: human knowledge relates as 'known', divine as 'knower', so they are not similar.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of Abu al-Husayn, what distinguishes divine knowledge from human knowledge even when they share the same object?
A) The object is different for each.
B) Human knowledge relates as 'known' while divine knowledge relates as 'knower'.
C) Human knowledge is detailed and divine knowledge is general.
D) The time of their relation differs.

Key Takeaway
The mode of relation (t'alluq) differentiates divine and human knowledge even when the object is the same.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge, do you unconsciously imagine it as a larger, more detailed version of your own? How might that subtle assumption keep you from truly revering Him as the All-Knowing? What can you do today to remind yourself that His mode of knowing is entirely different from yours?
?
This passage shows that even when God and you know the same fact (like 'Zaid is in the house'), the 'how' of that knowledge is incomparable. Have you ever tried to put yourself in God's place, imagining you know every detail as He does? What danger lies in that imagination, and how can you guard your heart from it?
?
The refutation hinges on the mode of relation – God relates as 'Knower', you as 'known'. In what area of your life are you most tempted to judge God's actions as if He were a human knower with limited perspective? Commit right now to recite one attribute of Allah (like al-'Alim) with the understanding that His knowledge is unlike any created knowledge.

Proofs of Similarity and Their Rebuttal
Two proofs for similarity of knowledges and their rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs offered by Abu al-Husayn to show that if two knowledges relate to the same object in the same mode they are similar, and the author's counter-arguments.

Core Idea
Similar knowledges are substitutable, but divine and human knowledge are not substitutable due to different essences.

After distinguishing the modes of relation, the author presents two arguments that would prove similarity if the mode were identical. He then shows why these arguments do not apply to divine knowledge. The first argument is about substitutability, the second about negation by a contrary belief. Understanding these proofs deepens the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the substitution proof
Read the first part of the passage where the author presents evidence that two knowledges relating to the same thing in the same limit are similar because each takes the place of the other.
Note the phrase 'سَدَّ مَسَدَّ الآخر'.
2
Read the negation proof
Read the second argument: if a contrary belief arises, it would negate both knowledges if they are similar. Understand why this shows similarity.
The author uses the example of blackness negating two whitenesses.
3
Understand why these proofs do not apply
The author implicitly rejects these arguments because the mode of relation differs, so the condition of 'same limit' is not met. Reflect on how the previous lesson's distinction prevents these proofs from being effective.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two knowledges are negated by the same doubt, they must be similar in essence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the negation argument not prove similarity between divine and human knowledge?
A) Because they have different objects.
B) Because divine knowledge is not present in the human heart.
C) Because time differs.

?

?

?
The rebuttal emphasizes the unique mode of divine knowledge. How does that reshape your attitude when you pray or make dua, knowing that Allah's knowledge is not subject to the same limitations as human knowing?
