Fourth Argument: Limited Similarity Only in Aspect of Relation to the Known
Nasafi argues that similarity, if proven, is confined to the aspect of relation to the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even if similarity between divine and human knowledge is granted, it is limited to the specific aspect of relation to the known, and does not extend to other attributes.

Core Idea
Similarity between God's knowledge and ours is strictly limited to their shared relation to the known.

This lesson presents the fourth argument from Imam Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla. Nasafi argues that even if one concedes that divine and human knowledge share the attribute of being related to the known, that does not imply they are similar in all respects. He demonstrates that reasoning by analogy must be restricted to the precise point of similarity, otherwise it leads to ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text and translation
Read the full Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the flow of the argument from premise to conclusion.
Notice how Nasafi builds the argument step by step.
2
Identify the key point
Pinpoint the core idea: similarity is confined to the specific attribute of relation to the known, not extended to other attributes like createdness or necessity.
This is the main lesson to take away.
3
Reflect on the logical principle
Consider why applying a general ruling from a specific cause is ignorance. This reinforces the importance of precision in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع: أن المماثلة لو ثبتت بين علم الباري سبحانه وتعالى وبين علمنا عنده لاشتراكهما في التعلق بالمعلوم على طريقة واحدة لكان ينبغي أن تقتصر المماثلة على الوصف الخاص الذي ثبت به الاشتراك بينهما ، وهو التعلق بالمعلوم فكانا مثلين من حيث التعلق بالمعلوم على طريقة واحدة، ولم يتعد المماثلة ما وراء ذلك مما لا اشتراك بينهما فيه من كون أحد العلمين محدثا وضروريا أو مكتسبا وعرضا مستحيل البقاء غير شامل على المعلومات والآخر مخالفا له في هذه الوجوه، فلا توجب المماثلة بينهما في هذه الوجوه، لانعدام دليل المماثلة (في حقها إذ دليل المماثلة) مقتصر على جهة التعلق فحسب، وهذا لأن أحد العلمين سد مسد صاحبه فيما يثبت الاشتراك بينهما لا فيما وراء ذلك، فكان إثبات الحكم عاما عند اختصاص علة الحكم ودليلة جهلا محضا. وبالله التوفيق.
The fourth: That if similarity were established between the knowledge of God, the Exalted, and our knowledge, according to him, due to their sharing in relation to the known in one manner, then it would be necessary that similarity be restricted to the specific attribute by which the sharing between them is established, namely relation to the known in one manner. So they would be similar in terms of relation to the known in one manner, and similarity would not extend beyond that to aspects in which there is no sharing between them, such as one of the two knowledges being originated and necessary, or acquired and an accident impossible to subsist, not encompassing all known things, while the other is contrary in these aspects. So similarity between them in these aspects is not necessitated, due to the absence of evidence for similarity with respect to them, since the evidence for similarity is confined solely to the aspect of relation. This is because one of the two knowledges takes the place of the other only in what establishes sharing between them, not beyond that. So establishing a general ruling when the cause and evidence of the ruling are specific is sheer ignorance. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument. It presents the fourth argument: even if one were to grant similarity between divine and human knowledge based on their shared relation to the known, this similarity would be limited to that specific aspect. It would not extend to other attributes like createdness, necessity, being an accident, etc. To apply similarity generally without evidence is ignorant.

Key Terms
مماثلة (similarity)
تعلق (relation)
معلوم (known)
علة (cause)
دليل (evidence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sharing one attribute (like relation to the known) makes two things similar in all attributes.
Correction: Nasafi shows that similarity is restricted to the specific shared attribute and does not extend to other aspects.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, to what aspect is the similarity between God's knowledge and human knowledge limited, if it were proven?
A) Their being accidents.
B) Their relation to the known in one manner.
C) Their being created.
D) Their encompassing all known things.

Key Takeaway
Similarity between divine and human knowledge, if granted, is limited only to the shared attribute of relation to the known; applying it elsewhere is unfounded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture God knowing your thoughts and actions, do you unconsciously imagine His knowledge working like a more powerful version of yours? How can you actively remind yourself that any similarity is only in the mere fact of 'knowing,' not in the nature of that knowledge?
?
This argument warns us not to extend a limited similarity into other attributes. Is there an area of your understanding of God where you have overapplied a human trait (e.g., feeling, waiting, forgetting) without evidence from revelation? What would it cost you to surrender that assumption?
?
The passage calls overextending similarity 'sheer ignorance.' Does this challenge any belief you hold about God that you have not actually verified from the Qur'an and Sunnah? What step can you take today to replace that assumption with submission to what God has truly described of Himself?

The Fourth Argument: Similarity Is Limited to the Shared Aspect
Al-Nasafi presents the fourth argument refuting similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning behind the fourth argument against similarity between divine and human knowledge, which shows that sharing one attribute does not imply full similarity.

Core Idea
Shared attributes don't force full similarity; evidence is limited to the shared aspect.

In this passage, Al-Nasafi refutes the claim that divine and human knowledge are similar. He argues that even if they share the attribute of relating to the known, this similarity is restricted to that aspect alone. The differences in other attributes prevent any claim of overall similarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the premise: if similarity is based on shared relation, it should be confined to that relation.
2
Identify the differences
Identify the differences listed between divine and human knowledge: one is originated, necessary/acquired, an accident, etc. Recognize that these are not shared.
3
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that similarity cannot be extended beyond the shared aspect. The cause (evidence) is specific, so the ruling must be specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع: أن المماثلة لو ثبتت بين علم الباري سبحانه وتعالى وبين علمنا عنده لاشتراكهما في التعلق بالمعلوم على طريقة واحدة لكان ينبغي أن تقتصر المماثلة على الوصف الخاص الذي ثبت به الاشتراك بينهما، وهو التعلق بالمعلوم فكانا مثلين من حيث التعلق بالمعلوم على طريقة واحدة، ولم يتعد المماثلة ما وراء ذلك مما لا اشتراك بينهما فيه من كون أحد العلمين محدثا وضروريا أو مكتسبا وعرضا مستحيل البقاء غير شامل على المعلومات والآخر مخالفا له في هذه الوجوه، فلا توجب المماثلة بينهما في هذه الوجوه، لانعدام دليل المماثلة في حقها إذ دليل المماثلة مقتصر على جهة التعلق فحسب، وهذا لأن أحد العلمين سد مسد صاحبه فيما يثبت الاشتراك بينهما لا فيما وراء ذلك، فكان إثبات الحكم عاما عند اختصاص علة الحكم ودليلة جهلا محضا. وبالله التوفيق.
ثم عند الأشعرية وإن تعلق علم الباري بالمعلوم تعلق العلوم، وكذا علمنا لا مماثلة بينهما، لما مر من أن المماثلة عندهم تثبت بالاشتراك في جميع الصفات، وقد انعدم. وعندنا التعلق وإن وجد لعلمنا، ولكن تعلق ثبت بكسبنا في الاستدلالي أو بمحض تخليق الباري في الضروري، وتعلق علمه تعالى لا بصنع أحد فلم تثبت المشاركة. وبالله التوفيق.
And the fourth: that similarity, if it were established between the knowledge of Allah, exalted and high, and our knowledge in His view due to their sharing in relation to the known in one manner, then it would be necessary that similarity be confined to the specific attribute by which the sharing between them is established, namely the relation to the known. So they would be similar in terms of relation to the known in one manner, and similarity would not extend beyond that to what there is no sharing between them in it, such as one of the two knowledges being originated and necessary or acquired and an accident impossible to subsist, not encompassing all known things, and the other being contrary to it in these aspects. So similarity is not entailed between them in these aspects, due to the absence of the evidence of similarity with respect to them, since the evidence of similarity is confined solely to the aspect of relation. And this is because one of the two knowledges takes the place of the other in what establishes sharing between them, not beyond that. So establishing the ruling generally when the cause of the ruling and its evidence are specific is pure ignorance. And with Allah is success.
Then, according to the Ash'ariyya, even if the knowledge of Allah relates to the known as knowledge relates, and likewise our knowledge, there is no similarity between them, because it has preceded that similarity with them is established by sharing in all attributes, and that is absent. And according to us, relation exists for our knowledge, but it is a relation established by our acquisition in inferential knowledge or by sheer creation of Allah in necessary knowledge, while the relation of His knowledge, exalted, is not by anyone's act, so sharing is not established. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the claim of similarity (mumathala) between God's knowledge and human knowledge. The argument proceeds by conceding that both knowledges share the aspect of relation (ta'alluq) to the known, but insists that similarity can only be asserted within that specific aspect. Beyond that, the two knowledges are radically different: human knowledge is created (muhdath), either necessary (daruri) or acquired (muktasab), an accident (arad) that cannot subsist, and does not encompass all known things; divine knowledge is eternal and opposite in all these respects. Therefore, the evidence of similarity (dalil al-mumathala) does not extend to those other aspects. The passage then contrasts the Ash'arite and Maturidite positions: for the Ash'arites, similarity requires complete sharing in all attributes, which is absent; for the Maturidites, even the relation differs because human knowledge's relation is either acquired or directly created by Allah, while divine knowledge's relation has no intermediary. Thus no participation (musharaka) is established.

Key Terms
المماثلة
التعلق
العلم
الاشتراك
المحدث
الضروري
المكتسب
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing one attribute implies full similarity.
Correction: Similarity is limited to that specific attribute; other attributes may differ, preventing overall similarity.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what is the consequence of divine and human knowledge sharing the aspect of relation to the known?
A) They are similar in all respects.
B) They are similar only in that aspect.
C) They are not similar at all.
D) They are identical.

Key Takeaway
Sharing one attribute does not make two things similar in all respects; the evidence for similarity is limited to the shared aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because someone shares one good quality with you, they must be like you in every way? How does this argument from Al-Nasafi expose the flaw in that assumption, and how can you apply this to avoid making unfair judgments about others?
?
If you acknowledge that Allah's knowledge is utterly different from yours in origin and nature, how should that affect the way you seek knowledge and depend on your own understanding? What one change can you make today to humble your intellect before His?
?
Does your heart sometimes fall into the trap of comparing Allah's attributes to human ones in a way that reduces His majesty? What specific step from this argument can you use to correct yourself when such thoughts arise?

Sectarian Differences on Divine Knowledge Similarity
Al-Nasafi compares Ash'ari and Maturidi views on similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools differ in their criteria for establishing similarity between divine and human knowledge.

Core Idea
Ash'aris require full attribute sharing; Maturidis see even the relation as fundamentally different.

After presenting the fourth argument, Al-Nasafi contrasts the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions. For the Ash'aris, similarity requires sharing all attributes, which is absent. For the Maturidis, even the shared relation differs because its cause is different: human knowledge's relation is acquired or created, divine knowledge's is uncreated.

Guided Path
1
Read the sectarian section
Read the Arabic passage and translation starting with 'ثم عند الأشعرية'. Note the two positions.
2
Compare the criteria
Ash'aris require complete similarity in all attributes; Maturidis focus on the nature of the relation.
3
Apply the argument
Explain why both schools reject full similarity despite the shared relation.

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the claim of similarity (mumathala) between God's knowledge and human knowledge. The argument proceeds by conceding that both knowledges share the aspect of relation (ta'alluq) to the known, but insists that similarity can only be asserted within that specific aspect. Beyond that, the two knowledges are radically different: human knowledge is created (muhdath), either necessary (daruri) or acquired (muktasab), an accident (arad) that cannot subsist, and does not encompass all known things; divine knowledge is eternal and opposite in all these respects. Therefore, the evidence of similarity (dalil al-mumathala) does not extend to those other aspects. The passage then contrasts the Ash'arite and Maturidite positions: for the Ash'arites, similarity requires complete sharing in all attributes, which is absent; for the Maturidites, even the relation differs because human knowledge's relation is either acquired or directly created by Allah, while divine knowledge's relation has no intermediary. Thus no participation (musharaka) is established.

Key Terms
الأشعرية
الماتريدية
المماثلة
التعلق
الاشتراك
الاستدلالي
الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris and Maturidis agree on the criteria for similarity.
Correction: Ash'aris require complete attribute sharing; Maturidis focus on the nature of the relation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do Ash'aris reject similarity between divine and human knowledge?
A) Because they share only one attribute.
B) Because similarity requires sharing all attributes, which is absent.
C) Because the relation is different.
D) Because human knowledge is created.

Key Takeaway
Ash'aris and Maturidis both reject full similarity but for different reasons: Ash'aris demand complete attribute sharing, while Maturidis see the relation itself as fundamentally different.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you compare yourself to others, do you, like the Ash'aris, demand total similarity in every attribute to claim equality, or do you, like the Maturidis, examine the very origin of your actions? Which approach humbles you more?
?
Your knowledge of Allah comes through inference (istidlali) or direct creation (daruri). Does that lead you to pride (your own kasb) or gratitude (His takhliq)? How does this lesson change your attitude towards your own understanding?
?
If divine knowledge's relation is entirely beyond your act, what does that say about your ability to truly 'know' Allah? How does this truth affect the way you seek Him—with intellectual striving or humble surrender?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Divine Knowledge Without an Attribute
Al-Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila with three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite view that God is 'alim solely by the description of others is rationally untenable, and how Al-Nasafi's three objections expose its absurdity.

Core Idea
Description alone cannot make something true; reality depends on an internal attribute.

The Mu'tazila claimed that God is described as knowing ('alim) even though He has no attribute of knowledge subsisting in Him. They argued that the description by a describer (wasf al-wasif) is sufficient. In this passage, Al-Nasafi dismantles this claim with three powerful objections, showing that it leads to absurdity and contradicts reason.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
Read the first line: the Mu'tazila say God is 'alim by the description of others. Grasp what this claim entails—no internal attribute.
2
Examine the first objection (absurd examples)
Al-Nasafi asks: if description alone suffices, then calling a black person white would make them white. This is obviously false and exposes the flaw.
3
Examine the second objection (extension to non-humans)
If description is enough, then calling a donkey or child 'alim would be truthful—another absurdity showing the view leads to pure sophistry.
4
Examine the third objection (pre-eternity)
In pre-eternity (azal), there were no describers. So according to the Mu'tazila, God would not have been 'alim then, which is impossible for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للمعتزلة - ما معنى قولكم: إن الله تعالى موصوف بأنه عالم إذا لم يكن له علم؟
فإن قالوا: موصوف بأنه عالم بوصف الواصفين إياه إذ الصفة الواصف إياه. قيل لهم لو أن رجلا قال لزنجي: إنه أبيض، أو لقصير: إنه طويل هل كان الزنجي أبيض والقصير طويلا؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، كابروا، وإن قالوا: لا، ناقضوا، لأن الوصف من الواصف قد وجد، ولا عبرة له عند انعدام المعنى القائم بالموصوف.
ثم يقال لهم: إذا كان من وصفه متكلم بأنه عالم، لا من قام به العلم أو من له العلم ينبغي أن يقال: إن من قال الحمار: إنه عالم، أو لطفل أو لمجنون لم يقم به علم بأنه عالم أن يكون صادقا في مقالته، لوجود الوصف له بذلك، إذ هو يكون عالما بوصف الواصف له بذلك لا بمعنى قائم به، وهذه مكابرة ظاهرة، وسوفسطائية محضة.
ثم يقال لهم ما معنى قولكم إن الله كان في الأزل موصوفا بكونه عالما والواصفون كانوا معدومين في الأزل فلا يتصور وجود كلامهم؟ وكلامه أيضا عندكم
And it is said to the Mu'tazila: What is the meaning of your statement that Allah, exalted, is described as knowing (ʿālim) when He has no knowledge (ʿilm)?
If they say: He is described as knowing by the description of the describers of Him, since the description is the describer of Him. It is said to them: If a man said to a black person: he is white, or to a short person: he is tall, would the black person be white and the short person tall? If they say yes, they commit stubbornness; if they say no, they contradict, because the description from the describer has occurred, and it is of no consideration when the meaning subsisting in the described is absent.
Then it is said to them: If the one who describes Him as knowing, not by virtue of a knowledge subsisting in Him or of Him having knowledge, then it should be said that someone who said of a donkey: it is knowing, or of a child or an insane person with whom no knowledge subsists, that he is truthful in his statement, because of the description of him by that, since it would be knowing by the description of the describer, not by a meaning subsisting in it. And this is manifest obstinacy and pure sophistry.
Then it is said to them: What is the meaning of your statement that Allah was in pre-eternity described as being knowing when the describers were non-existent in pre-eternity, so the existence of their speech cannot be conceived? And His speech also, according to you
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that God is described as 'alim (knowing) without a subsisting attribute of knowledge. The Mu'tazila argue that the description by the describer (wasf al-wasif) is sufficient. Al-Nasafi responds with three objections: (1) If description alone were sufficient, then calling a black person white or a short person tall would make them so—this is absurd and contradictory. (2) If a person describes a donkey, child, or insane person as 'alim, they would have to be considered truthful, which is manifest obstinacy (mukabara) and pure sophistry (sawfasta'iyya). (3) In pre-eternity (azal), there were no describers, so according to the Mu'tazila, God would not have been 'alim then, which is impossible. The passage thus demonstrates the necessity of an internal attribute of knowledge subsisting in God.

عالم

وصف

سوفسطائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can be truly described as 'alim solely by external description, even without an internal attribute of knowledge.
Correction: Description alone does not confer reality; an internal attribute (ma'na) subsisting in the described is necessary for a true description.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against the Mu'tazilite claim that God is 'alim by the description of others?
A) It contradicts the Quran.
B) It would mean calling a black person white would make them white.
C) It requires an eternal attribute.
D) It denies God's speech.

Key Takeaway
True description of God as 'alim requires an attribute of knowledge subsisting in Him; external description alone is insufficient and leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a false belief about Allah by saying 'it's just how we describe Him' without considering whether the description corresponds to reality? In what specific area of your faith might you be doing this now—what will you do today to correct it?
?
If you were to describe someone as knowledgeable when they clearly are not, would you consider yourself truthful? Why then do you accept a similar claim about Allah if it contradicts His perfection? Be honest: which of Allah's attributes do you struggle to accept as real because you cannot reconcile them in your mind?
?
The Mu'tazila feared ascribing multiplicity to Allah. But does your fear of 'overcomplicating' Allah prevent you from submitting to what He has said about Himself? What attribute do you need to affirm today with full conviction, even if others accuse you of anthropomorphism or excess?

Divine and Human Knowledge: Similarity or Not?
Al-Nasafi compares Ash'ari and Maturidi views on divine knowledge and attribute similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi distinguishes the Maturidi position from the Ash'ari position on the relation between divine and human knowledge and the concept of similarity.

Core Idea
Divine and human knowledge are not similar because similarity requires complete sharing of attributes.

In this passage, al-Nasafi presents a concise comparison between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools on a delicate point: how does God's knowledge relate to human knowledge? Both schools affirm that God knows, but they differ on whether there is any similarity (mumathala) between divine and human knowledge. The author explains the Ash'ari stance and then presents the Maturidi view, grounding it in the concepts of kasb and takhliq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once without analyzing deeply. Focus on identifying the two schools' positions.
2
Compare the two positions
Identify the Ash'ari argument (no similarity because sharing all attributes is absent) and the Maturidi argument (our knowledge is acquired or created, God's is not, so no sharing).
Notice how each school uses different premises to reach a similar conclusion.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on mumathala, kasb, takhliq, and musharaka. Understand how these terms shape the argument for each school.
Mumathala requires identity in all attributes, not just analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عند الأشعرية وإن تعلق علم الباري بالمعلوم تعلق العلوم، وكذا علمنا لا مماثلة بينهما، لما مر من أن المماثلة عندهم تثبت بالاشتراك في جميع الصفات، وقد انعدم. وعندنا التعلق وإن وجد لعلمنا، ولكن تعلق ثبت بكسبنا في الاستدلالي أو بمحض تخليق الباري في الضروري، وتعلق علمه تعالى لا بصنع أحد فلم تثبت المشاركة. وبالله التوفيق.
Then according to the Ash'aris, even if the knowledge of the Creator relates to the known as knowledges relate, and similarly our knowledge, there is no similarity (mumathala) between them, because, as previously mentioned, similarity with them is established by sharing in all attributes, and that is absent. And according to us, the relation exists for our knowledge, but it is established by our acquisition (kasb) in inferential [knowledge] or by the sheer creation (takhliq) of the Creator in necessary [knowledge], while the relation of His knowledge, exalted, is not by anyone's act, so sharing (musharaka) is not established. And by God is success.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a comparative theological argument distinguishing the Maturidi position from the Ash'ari position on divine knowledge. It begins by stating the Ash'ari view: although God's knowledge relates to what is known in the same way that knowledges relate (i.e., it is a true knowledge that corresponds to its object), and similarly our knowledge does so, there is no similarity (mumathala) between God's knowledge and human knowledge because, for the Ash'aris, similarity would require complete sharing in all attributes, which is impossible. Then the Maturidi position is given: we (Maturidis) agree that there is a relation for our knowledge, but this relation is established either by our own acquisition (kasb) in the case of inferential knowledge, or by God's direct creation (takhliq) in the case of necessary knowledge. Meanwhile, the relation of God's knowledge is not caused by any creature's act, so no sharing is established. The author concludes with a prayer for divine success.

Key Terms
علم الباري
مماثلة
كسب
تخليق
مشاركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similarity (mumathala) between divine and human knowledge is possible because both are 'knowledge'.
Correction: For Ash'aris, similarity requires sharing all attributes, which is impossible between Creator and creature. Maturidis use a different argument based on causal origin.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the basis for the Ash'ari denial of similarity between divine and human knowledge?
A) They deny that God has knowledge.
B) Similarity requires sharing in all attributes, which is impossible between Creator and creature.
C) Human knowledge is acquired, while divine knowledge is not.
D) They follow the Mu'tazila in this matter.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi shows that while both schools deny full similarity between divine and human knowledge, they do so for different reasons: Ash'aris because of the nature of similarity, Maturidis because of the difference in causal origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study Islam and gain knowledge, do you secretly consider that knowledge as your own achievement? How does the Maturidi teaching that even your inferential knowledge is ultimately created by God (through takhliq or with your kasb) force you to reconsider your sense of intellectual independence?
?
If divine knowledge and human knowledge are completely unlike, what does that imply about your efforts to know God? Are you trying to comprehend something that you can never fully grasp? How does that affect your humility in worship?
?
The Ash'aris say no similarity because sharing all attributes is impossible. Reflect on your own tendency to anthropomorphize God when you think about His attributes. Which of God's attributes do you most struggle to think of as completely unlike yours? How can this lesson correct that impulse?

God's Knowledge Cannot Be Merely Descriptive
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God is 'alim by description only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that God is 'alim by description alone leads to absurdity and how al-Nasafi refutes it using logical analogies.

Core Idea
Descriptions without real attributes lead to absurd contradictions.

Al-Nasafi challenges the Mu'tazilite position that God can be called 'knowing' without having an attribute of knowledge. He uses a simple analogy: calling a black person white does not make them white, just as describing God without a real attribute is meaningless. This lesson walks through his argument and its implications for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
Recognize that the Mu'tazila say God is 'alim only by the description of describers, not by a subsisting attribute. This is the target of refutation.
2
Follow the first analogy
Al-Nasafi compares this to calling a black person white or a short person tall. See why the description alone fails to change reality.
3
Examine the reductio ad absurdum
The author then extends the logic: if description sufficed, calling a donkey 'alim would make it knowing. This shows the view is sophistry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للمعتزلة - ما معنى قولكم: إن الله تعالى موصوف بأنه عالم إذا لم يكن له علم؟ فإن قالوا: موصوف بأنه عالم بوصف الواصفين إياه إذ الصفة الواصف إياه. قيل لهم لو أن رجلا قال لزنجي: إنه أبيض، أو لقصير: إنه طويل هل كان الزنجي أبيض والقصير طويلا ؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، كابروا، وإن قالوا: لا، ناقضوا، لأن الوصف من الواصف قد وجد، ولا عبرة له عند انعدام المعنى القائم بالموصوف. ثم يقال لهم: إذا كان من وصفه متكلم بأنه عالم، لا من قام به العلم أو من له العلم ينبغي أن يقال : إن من قال الحمار : إنه عالم، أو لطفل أو لمجنون لم يقم به علم بأنه عالم أن يكون صادقا في مقالته، لوجود الوصف له بذلك، إذ هو يكون عالما بوصف الواصف له بذلك لا بمعنى قائم به، وهذه مكابرة ظاهرة، وسوفسطائية محضة.
And it is said to the Mu'tazila: What is the meaning of your statement that God, exalted, is described as knowing ('alim) when He has no knowledge? If they say: He is described as knowing by the description of the describers of Him, for the description is the describer's description of him. It is said to them: If a man said to a black person: 'He is white,' or to a short person: 'He is tall,' would the black person be white and the short person tall? If they say: Yes, they contradict reality; if they say: No, they contradict themselves, because the description from the describer has occurred, and it is of no consequence when the meaning subsisting in the described is absent. Then it is said to them: If one who describes him as speaking is said to be knowing, not one in whom knowledge subsists or who has knowledge, it would be necessary that if someone says of a donkey: 'He is knowing,' or of a child or a madman who has no knowledge subsisting in him, that he would be truthful in his statement, because the description is made of him thereby, so he would be knowing by the description of the describer of him as such, not by a meaning subsisting in him. This is clear outlandishness (mukabara) and pure sophistry (sufistaiyya).
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that God can be described as 'alim (knowing) without having an attribute of knowledge subsisting in Him. The author begins by posing a challenge: what does it mean to say God is described as knowing if He has no knowledge? He then anticipates their answer: they claim He is described as knowing by the description of describers—i.e., people attribute knowledge to Him. To refute this, al-Nasafi uses a reductio ad absurdum: if someone calls a black person white or a short person tall, the description alone does not make them so. The describer's description has no effect when the actual quality is absent. Thus, if the Mu'tazila admit this, they contradict their own principle; if they deny it, they are being obstinate. The author then strengthens the argument with another case: if the validity of a description depended solely on the describer, then calling a donkey, a child, or a madman 'alim would be true, which is absurd. He concludes that such a view is clear outlandishness (mukabara) and pure sophistry (sufistaiyya), exposing its irrationality.

Key Terms
موصوف
وصف الواصفين
مكابرة
سوفسطائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Describing God as 'alim is merely a linguistic convention without any real attribute.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that if description alone sufficed, absurd consequences follow, proving that descriptions must correspond to real attributes.

Checkpoint
What example does al-Nasafi give to show the absurdity of the Mu'tazilite claim that God is 'alim by description alone?
A) A man calling a donkey 'learned'.
B) A man calling a stone 'living'.
C) A man calling a tree 'seeing'.
D) A man calling the sky 'sweet'.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite view by showing that relying solely on descriptions leads to absurdities, thus affirming that God's attribute of knowledge must be a real subsisting attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Pre-Eternal Description Requires Pre-Eternal Describers
Al-Nasafi argues against the Mu'tazilite claim of pre-eternal description without describers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that God cannot be described as knowing in pre-eternity without an eternal describer.

Core Idea
Without a describer, no description—even God's—can occur in pre-eternity.

This passage presents a critical argument against the Mu'tazilite view that God was described as knowing by His own essence in pre-eternity. Al-Nasafi challenges them: if no describers existed and God's speech is created, how could God ever be described as knowing? The answer exposes a contradiction in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the rhetorical question style.
2
Identify the two prongs of the argument
The argument has two parts: first, human describers did not exist in pre-eternity, so their speech is impossible; second, God's speech is created, so He did not describe Himself either. Find both in the text.
3
Examine the conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazilite claim is false and baseless. Observe how he connects his premises to this conclusion.
The conclusion goes beyond just 'knowing' to include all eternal attributes.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
This argument defends the reality of eternal attributes against a rationalist objection. Ask yourself: why is this important for Islamic theology?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم ما معنى قولكم إن الله كان في الأزل موصوفا بكونه عالما والواصفون كانوا معدومين في الأزل فلا يتصور وجود كلامهم؟ وكلامه أيضا عندكم محدث مخلوق، فلم يوجد منه وصف نفسه بأنه عالم. فكان قولهم: إنه كان في الأزل موصوفا بأنه حي قادر باق سميع بصير، باطلا لا تحقق له. وبالله التوفيق.
Then it is said to them: What is the meaning of your statement that God was in pre-eternity (al-azal) described as being knowing when the describers were non-existent in pre-eternity, so their speech cannot be conceived? And His speech also, according to you, is originated (muhdath) and created (makhluq), so there did not occur from Him a description of Himself as knowing. So their statement that He was in pre-eternity described as living, powerful, enduring, hearing, seeing is false, having no reality. And by God is success.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against the Mu'tazilite position that God is described as knowing by the description of others. The author asks: how can you claim God was described as knowing in pre-eternity (al-azal) when the describers (i.e., creatures) did not exist at that time? Their speech could not possibly exist. Furthermore, according to the Mu'tazila themselves, God's speech (i.e., the Qur'an) is created and originated, not eternal, so God never described Himself as knowing in pre-eternity either. Consequently, the Mu'tazilite claim that God was in pre-eternity described as living, powerful, enduring, hearing, seeing is baseless and has no reality. The author concludes with a prayer for divine success.

الأزل
موصوف

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Al-Nasafi shows it requires either an eternal describer or self-description, both of which Mu'tazila deny.

Checkpoint

Al-Nasafi demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite claim of God being described in pre-eternity is self-contradictory because no describers existed and God did not describe Himself.

Exposing the Mu'tazilite Aim: Denial of Divine Attributes
Al-Nasafi reveals that Mu'tazila's real goal is to deny God's attributes outright.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi exposes the Mu'tazilite as secretly denying divine attributes while outwardly affirming the names.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila kept the names but denied the reality of divine attributes.

Al-Nasafi concludes his critique of the Mu'tazila by revealing their true intention: to deny that God has real attributes such as life, power, and knowledge. He argues that they hid this view to avoid persecution and outwardly used the divine names while interpreting them away. In this passage, he names specific Mu'tazilite figures and exposes their strategy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the accusatory tone and the names mentioned.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the central accusation
Al-Nasafi claims the Mu'tazila agreed with philosophers in affirming a bare eternal essence without attributes. Identify that sentence.
Look for 'موافقة إخوانهم من الفلاسفة'.
3
Understand why they hid their view
The passage says they feared 'the shame of the sword'—i.e., persecution by Sunni authorities. Note this reason.
4
Examine the examples given
Al-Nasafi cites al-Nashi', 'Abbad ibn Sulayman al-Saymari, and al-Salihi. For each, see how they reinterpreted the attribute names to negate their meanings.
Al-Salihi said 'He is knowing' means only 'He is a thing unlike things.'
5
Reflect on 'more fortunate' remark
Al-Nasafi says these individuals are 'more fortunate' because they persisted in qiyas (analogical inference) in the name itself. What does this imply?
They at least kept the verbal form, unlike other Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن وقف على ما ثبت في المسألة من الدلائل والكشف لتمويهات المعتزلة، عرف أن القوم لم يريدوا بما ذهبوا إليه إلا موافقة إخوانهم من الفلاسفة، في إثبات ذات في القديم ليس بحي ولا قادر ولا عالم ولا سميع ولا بصير غير أنهم لم يتجاسروا على إظهار ذلك خوفا من معرة السيف، لما فيه من جحود ما ورد به الكتاب والسنة المتواترة، فأطلقوا هذه الأسامي في الظاهر وأتوا بما يؤدي إلى مقصودهم، من نفي كونه حيا قادرا سميعا بصيرا، وقد صرح بعضهم أن مرادهم هذا وهو الناشي على أحد قوليه على ما مر وقد روي عن عباد بن سليمان الصيمري مثل مقالة الناشي. وزعم الصالحي أنه إذا قيل: إنه تعالى عالم كان معناه أنه شيء لا كالأشياء، وهذا رجوع إلى نفي الوصف له بأنه عالم. وهؤلاء أسعد حالا من بقية المعتزلة، حيث استمروا على القياس في نفس الاسم
Whoever stops to consider what has been established in the matter from the evidences and the unveiling of the deceptions (tamwiyyat) of the Mu'tazila knows that these people intended nothing by what they went to except agreement with their brothers the philosophers in affirming a pre-eternal essence that is not living, not powerful, not knowing, not hearing, not seeing, except that they did not dare to show that out of fear of the shame of the sword, because of what it involves of denying what the Book and the recurrent Sunna have brought. So they outwardly uttered these names and brought what leads to their intended goal of negating that He is living, powerful, hearing, seeing. And some of them explicitly stated that this is their intent, namely al-Nashi' in one of his two opinions as previously mentioned, and it has been narrated from 'Abbad ibn Sulayman al-Saymari a statement like the statement of al-Nashi'. And al-Salihi claimed that when it is said: He, exalted, is knowing, its meaning is that He is a thing unlike things, and this is a return to negating the description of Him as knowing. And these are more fortunate than the rest of the Mu'tazila, since they persisted in the analogical inference (qiyas) in the name itself.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage serves as a concluding expose by al-Nasafi, summarizing his critique of the Mu'tazilite position on divine attributes. He states that anyone who examines the proofs and sees through the deceptions of the Mu'tazila will realize that their real goal is not the mere negation of eternal attributes but rather agreement with the philosophers in affirming an eternal essence devoid of life, power, knowledge, hearing, and sight. They hid this view out of fear of the sword (i.e., persecution by Sunni authorities) because openly denying these attributes would contradict the Qur'an and mutawatir Sunna. Thus, they outwardly used the names (like 'alim, qadir) but introduced interpretations that effectively negate the attributes. Al-Nasafi provides specific examples: al-Nashi' (a known Mu'tazilite) openly stated in one of his opinions that this was his intent, and similarly 'Abbad ibn Sulayman al-Saymari held the same. Al-Salihi claimed that saying 'God is knowing' only means 'He is a thing unlike things,' which reduces the attribute to a negation. Al-Nasafi remarks that these individuals (al-Nashi', etc.) are more fortunate than the rest of the Mu'tazila because they at least maintained the analogical inference (qiyas) in the name itself—i.e., they kept the verbal form of the attribute, even if they reinterpreted it.

Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila merely differ on interpretation of divine attributes while still affirming them in some sense.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues they secretly deny the attributes altogether, aligning with philosophers who affirm a bare essence. Their outward use of names is a deceptive tactic.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what was the real reason the Mu'tazila did not openly deny God's attributes?
A) They were unsure of their position.
B) Fear of the sword (persecution).
C) They actually believed in the attributes.
D) They wanted to avoid contradicting philosophers.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi reveals that the Mu'tazilite's true intent is to deny God's attributes by reinterpreting the divine names, a strategy hidden to avoid persecution.

?
Al-Nasafi exposes that some scholars outwardly use divine names but drain them of meaning. In your own worship, do you ever repeat the names of Allah without pondering their realities? Do you live as if Allah is truly Living, Knowing, Seeing, or sometimes as if He is a distant abstraction? What would change if you took these attributes seriously every moment?

The Mu'tazilites' Hidden Agreement with the Philosophers
Nasafi exposes the Mu'tazilites' true intention in denying God's real attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilites outwardly affirmed God's names while secretly denying His real attributes, aligning with the philosophers.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites hid their denial of attributes behind empty names.

In this passage, Nasafi reveals the hidden agenda of the Mu'tazilites. He argues that their denial of real attributes is not based on reason alone but is a deliberate attempt to align with atheist philosophers. They feared the sword, so they kept the names but emptied them of meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice Nasafi's strong language: 'deceptions' and 'they did not dare to reveal.'
This sets the tone for the refutation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite intention
Nasafi says they intended to agree with philosophers in affirming an essence without attributes. What is the philosopher's position?
The philosophers affirm a bare essence (ذات) without attributes like life, power, knowledge.
3
Understand the fear factor
Why did they not openly deny attributes? Because of fear of the sword from the Muslim community. So they used names outwardly.
This shows their duplicity.
4
Connect to the lesson objective
Reflect on how this exposes the true Mu'tazilite position: they negate the reality of attributes while keeping the names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن وقف على ما ثبت في المسألة من الدلائل والكشف لتمويهات المعتزلة، عرف أن القوم لم يريدوا بما ذهبوا إليه إلا موافقة إخوانهم من الفلاسفة، في إثبات ذات في القديم ليس بحي ولا قادر ولا عالم ولا سميع ولا بصير غير أنهم لم يتجاسروا على إظهار ذلك خوفا من معرة السيف، لما فيه من جحود ما ورد به الكتاب والسنة المتواترة، فأطلقوا هذه الأسامي في الظاهر وأتوا بما يؤدي إلى مقصودهم، من نفي كونه حيا قادرا سميعا بصيرا، وقد صرح بعضهم أن مرادهم هذا وهو الناشي على أحد قوليه على ما مر وقد روي عن عباد بن سليمان الصيمري مثل مقالة الناشي. وزعم الصالحي أنه إذا قيل: إنه تعالى عالم كان معناه أنه شيء لا كالأشياء، وهذا رجوع إلى نفي الوصف له بأنه عالم. وهؤلاء أسعد حالا من بقية المعتزلة، حيث استمروا على القياس في نفس الاسم المقدر على المعنى عن الذات الذي يستحيل عليه المعنى، قياسا على اسم المتحرك والساكن والأسود والأبيض، وغير ذلك من الصفات وغيرهم من عامة المعتزلة ناقضوا فأثبتوا الاسم مع استحالة ثبوت المعنى في الغائب، ونفوا في الشاهد عند عدم المعنى . والله الموفق.
Whoever examines what has been established in the matter of the proofs and the uncovering of the deceptions of the Mu'tazilites will know that the people (the Mu'tazilites) intended by what they went to nothing other than agreement with their brothers among the philosophers, in affirming an essence in pre-eternity that is not living, not powerful, not knowing, not hearing, not seeing, except that they did not dare to reveal that out of fear of the disgrace of the sword, because of the denial of what the Book and the widely transmitted Sunnah have brought. So they uttered these names outwardly and brought what leads to their purpose, namely negating that He is living, powerful, hearing, seeing. And some of them declared that this is their intention, namely al-Nashi' according to one of his two statements as has passed, and it has been narrated from 'Abbad ibn Sulayman al-Saymari like the statement of al-Nashi'. And al-Salihi claimed that if it is said: He, exalted, is knowing, its meaning is that He is a thing not like things, and this is a return to negating the description of Him as knowing. These are more fortunate than the rest of the Mu'tazilites, because they continued the analogy regarding the name itself that is abstracted from the meaning, from the essence that is impossible to have the meaning, analogous to the name 'moving' and 'stationary' and 'black' and 'white' and other attributes. And others among the general Mu'tazilites contradicted, so they affirmed the name while the meaning's subsistence in the absent is impossible, and they negated it in the witnessed when the meaning is absent. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on God's attributes. Nasafi begins by stating that whoever studies the proofs and the exposé of Mu'tazilite deceptions will realize that the Mu'tazilites actually intend to agree with the philosophers in affirming an eternal essence that is not living, powerful, knowing, hearing, or seeing. They conceal this out of fear of the sword, because the Quran and Sunnah explicitly affirm these attributes. So they outwardly use the names while actually negating the meanings. He then gives examples: al-Nashi' explicitly stated that this is his intention, and 'Abbad ibn Sulayman al-Saymari similarly held this view. Al-Salihi claimed that saying God is knowing means He is a thing not like things, which is effectively a negation of the attribute. Nasafi then compares the different groups: those like al-Nashi' and al-Salihi are better off because they consistently apply the analogy of names like 'moving' and 'stationary' – they affirm the name but deny the meaning, while the general Mu'tazilites are inconsistent: they affirm the name in the absent (God) while denying the meaning, but in the witnessed (creatures) they deny the name when the meaning is absent. This inconsistency is a contradiction. The passage ends with a prayer for divine assistance.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الفلاسفة
الصفات
الاسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites truly affirm that God has attributes because they use names like 'Living' and 'Knowing.'
Correction: Nasafi shows they only use the names outwardly; they deny the reality of the attributes themselves, agreeing with philosophers who posit a bare essence.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what was the Mu'tazilites' real intention in their doctrine of attributes?
A) To affirm that God is truly knowing, powerful, etc.
B) To agree with the philosophers in affirming an essence without attributes while outwardly using names.
C) To establish a middle ground between anthropomorphism and negation.
D) To reject the Quran and Sunnah openly.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites outwardly used God's names while secretly denying the reality of His attributes, hiding their true agreement with the philosophers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you used religious language publicly while your heart was distant from its meaning? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of such hypocrisy? Commit today to aligning your speech with your inner conviction.

The Mu'tazilites feared the sword, so they concealed their true belief. What fears cause you to hide or compromise your convictions about God's attributes? How can you overcome that fear to be truthful with Him?
?
Nasafi exposes the Mu'tazilites' hidden agreement with the philosophers. Are there areas in your own theology where you unknowingly agree with ideas that contradict the Quran and Sunnah? What step will you take this week to examine your beliefs against revelation?

The Inconsistency of Mu'tazilite Attribute Denial
Nasafi contrasts consistent and inconsistent Mu'tazilite groups on attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the inconsistency among Mu'tazilite groups in their application of the principle of 'name without meaning' between God and creatures.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites contradict themselves by treating God and creatures differently.

After exposing the Mu'tazilite deceit, Nasafi contrasts the positions of different Mu'tazilite figures. Al-Nashi' and al-Salihi are more consistent because they apply the same logic to both God and creatures: they affirm a name but deny its meaning. The majority, however, are inconsistent: they affirm names for God without meaning but deny names for creatures when meaning is absent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the figures
Note the names: al-Nashi', 'Abbad ibn Sulayman al-Saymari, al-Salihi. What did each say?
Al-Nashi' explicitly stated the intention; al-Salihi reinterpreted 'knowing' as 'a thing not like things.'
2
Understand the analogy used
Nasafi mentions the analogy of names like 'moving' and 'stationary.' In creatures, these names derive from a meaning (motion/stillness). But for God, they deny the meaning.
Those like al-Nashi' consistently affirm the name without meaning even in the witnessed (creatures).
3
Spot the inconsistency
The general Mu'tazilites affirm names for God (like 'Knowing') without meaning, but in the witnessed (creatures) they deny the name when the meaning is absent. This is contradictory.
Why would they affirm 'Knowing' for God if no knowledge exists, but deny 'Moving' for a stone if no motion exists?
4
Reflect on the lesson objective
This inconsistency undermines their position. Nasafi uses it to argue that their whole approach is flawed.

Why 'Indwelling' Is Inapplicable to God's Attributes
Nasafi explains why 'indwelling' cannot be used for God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'indwelling' (حلول) cannot be used for God's attributes, and how linguistic precision is required in theological discourse.

Core Idea
Indwelling implies physical stability, impossible for God.

Nasafi carefully examines the term 'indwelling' (حلول) used in everyday language for physical things. He shows that this term carries connotations of settling and rest, which are properties of bodies. Even when used metaphorically for physical attributes like blackness, it is only a loose expression. For God, such language must be avoided entirely, not even metaphorically, to prevent any suggestion of error.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without the translation. Notice the key term حلول and its derivatives.
The passage begins with 'ثم اعلم' indicating a new piece of knowledge.
2
Understand the linguistic basis
Nasafi defines حلول as السكون والاستقرار (stillness and settledness). Reflect why this is problematic for God.
3
Examine the analogy with physical attributes
The passage notes that even for bodies, we only say 'blackness indwells the body' metaphorically (على التوسع). Consider why.
4
Apply the prohibition to God
For God's attributes, the term is forbidden even metaphorically because of the obligation to avoid any expression that could imply error.
The passage ends with a statement about necessity of avoidance.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this lesson illustrates the Maturidi method of using language precision to protect divine transcendence.
This is a key example of kalam's attention to terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن علم الباري عز وجل لا يقال : إنه حل ذاته، ولا يقال: ذاته محل للعلم ولما وراءه من الصفات إن كانت موجودة به لما أن الحلول هو السكون والاستقرار، يقال: حل فلان بمحل كذا وارتحل والمحلة السكن، والصفة لا توصف بالسكون ، وكذا هذا في صفات الأجسام، لا يقال: حل السواد الجسم إلا على التوسع، ولا يستعمل ذلك في صفاته تعالى على التوسع لوجوب الاجتناب في الخطأ حسب وجوب الاجتناب عما يوهم صفاته عما الخطأ. يوجب
Then know that it is not said concerning the knowledge of God, Blessed and Exalted, that it indwells His essence, nor is it said that His essence is a place for knowledge and for other attributes, if they exist by it, because indwelling (al-hulul) is settling and stability. It is said: 'So-and-so alighted at such-and-such a place and departed,' and 'the abode' (al-mahalla) is a dwelling. The attribute is not described with stability, and similarly this applies to the attributes of bodies: it is not said, 'blackness indwells the body' except metaphorically. And it is not used for His attributes, Exalted, even metaphorically, because it is obligatory to avoid error, just as it is obligatory to avoid what suggests His attributes [something] that necessitates error. It necessitates.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and theological clarification. Nasafi begins by stating that we should not say that God's knowledge 'indwells' (حل) His essence, nor that His essence is a 'place' (محل) for knowledge or other attributes. The reason is that the term 'indwelling' (حلول) in Arabic implies physical settling and rest (سكون واستقرار), as in 'so-and-so alighted at a place.' Attributes are not described with such physical properties. The same applies to the attributes of bodies: we do not say 'blackness indwells the body' except metaphorically. For God's attributes, we cannot use such language even metaphorically because it is obligatory to avoid any expression that might imply error or suggest that God's attributes are like accidents in bodies. The passage ends abruptly with a statement about necessity of avoidance.

Key Terms
حلول
محل
سكون
استقرار
توسع
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that saying 'knowledge indwells God' is acceptable because it is a spiritual concept.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the word 'indwelling' literally implies physical stability and location, which is impossible for God. Even metaphorical use is avoided to prevent any misunderstanding.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi forbid saying that God's knowledge 'indwells' (يحل) His essence?
A) Because knowledge is not a body.
B) Because 'indwelling' implies rest and stability, which are physical properties.
C) Because the Quran does not use this term.
D) Because the Mu'tazilites use it.

Key Takeaway
The term 'indwelling' (حلول) cannot be applied to God's attributes because it implies physical stability, which is incompatible with divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you think of God's knowledge 'entering' or 'settling' in His essence, can you see how that image makes God dependent on something else? What must you actively replace that picture with?
?
This passage says it's obligatory to avoid even a suggestion of error in God's attributes. Is there an area of your belief or speech about God where you've been careless? What step will you take today to correct it?

Correct Terminology for God's Knowledge: Existing by His Essence
Nasafi discusses proper phrase for God's knowledge: 'existing by His essence' vs 'subsisting in it'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari preferred 'existing by His essence' over 'subsisting in His essence' for God's knowledge and which phrases to avoid.

Core Idea
Use literal terms for God's attributes; avoid metaphors that imply distinction.

This passage addresses a subtle but crucial point in Islamic theology: how to speak correctly about God's attributes. The early scholars used a phrase that Ash'ari found inaccurate. Here, Nasafi explains the reasoning behind the preferred terminology and warns against several expressions that could imply false ideas about the relationship between God and His attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the key terms: 'qaim bi-dhatihi' and 'mawjud bi-dhatihi'.
2
Understand Ash'ari's reasoning
Ash'ari rejected 'qaim bi-dhatihi' because the word 'qiyam' (subsistence) is metaphorical when applied to attributes, while 'wujud' (existence) is literal. The literal term is always preferred.
This is a principle in kalam: use clear, unambiguous language about God.
3
Identify the four forbidden phrases
The passage lists four phrases that must be avoided: 'with Him', 'in Him', 'adjacent to Him', 'separate from Him'. For each, note why it is wrong (e.g., implies spatial relationship or distinction).
These phrases are used for distinct objects, but God's attributes are not distinct from His essence.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider why it is obligatory to avoid such phrases. The principle: attributes are neither identical nor separate from the essence; they exist by it. Language must reflect that.
This safeguards tawhid and avoids anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن متقدمي أصحاب الصفات يطلقون أن علمه قائم بذاته، وأبو الحسن الأشعري لم يرض بهذه العبارة وقال : إن علمه تعالى موجود بذاته تعالى لما أن لفظة القيام في الصفات مجاز ولفظة الوجود حقيقة، وأعلم أنه لا يقال إن علمه تعالى معه لأنه ليس بقائم بنفسه فيكون معه، ولا يقال: هو فيه لأنه تعالى ليس بظرف للعلم، والعلم أيضا ليس بمتمكن فيه، ولا يقال : إنه مجاور له لأنه غير مماس له لما أنه لا يقبل المماسة، ولا أنه مباين له لما أنه لا يقبل المفارقة، ولما أن هذه الألفاظ مستعملة في المتغايرات ولا تغاير فيما نحن فيه ووجوب الامتناع عن إطلاق هذه العبارات في مع الموصوفات في الشاهد للمعنى الأول دون الثاني.
Then indeed, the early proponents of the attributes hold that His knowledge subsists in His essence (qa'im bi-dhatihi). Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari was not pleased with this expression and said: His knowledge, Exalted, exists by His essence (mawjud bi-dhatihi) because the word 'subsistence' (al-qiyam) regarding the attributes is metaphorical, while the word 'existence' (al-wujud) is literal. And know that it is not said that His knowledge is 'with Him' because it does not subsist by itself to be 'with Him'; nor is it said that it is 'in Him' because He, Exalted, is not a container for knowledge, and knowledge also is not located in Him; nor is it said that it is 'adjacent to Him' because it is not contiguous with Him, as He does not accept contiguity; nor that it is 'separate from Him' because it does not accept separation. And these expressions are used for mutually distinct things, and there is no mutual distinction in what we are discussing. And it is obligatory to refrain from using these expressions regarding the described attributes in the witnessed for the first meaning, not the second.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage discusses the correct terminology for God's knowledge. The early practitioners of the science of attributes used to say that God's knowledge 'subsists in His essence' (قائم بذاته). However, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari rejected this phrase because 'subsistence' (قيام) when applied to attributes is metaphorical, whereas 'existence' (وجود) is literal. Therefore, one should say that God's knowledge 'exists by His essence' (موجود بذاته). Nasafi then lists other phrases that must be avoided: one should not say that knowledge is 'with Him' (معه) because that implies it is an independent entity; nor 'in Him' (فيه) because that would make God a container; nor 'adjacent to Him' (مجاور له) because that implies spatial proximity; nor 'separate from Him' (مباين له) because that implies separation. All these expressions are used for things that are distinct from each other, but God's attributes are not distinct from His essence in the sense of being separate entities. Therefore, such language is inappropriate. Nasafi concludes by stating that it is obligatory to refrain from using these expressions for God's attributes, just as we avoid them in the physical world when there is no distinction.

Key Terms
قائم بذاته
موجود بذاته
قيام
وجود
حقيقة
مجاز
مغايرة
ظرف
مماسة
مفارقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is acceptable to say God's knowledge subsists in His essence (قائم بذاته).
Correction: Ash'ari deemed 'subsistence' metaphorical; the literal term is 'existing by His essence' (موجود بذاته). Other phrases like 'with Him', 'in Him', 'adjacent', 'separate' are all inappropriate because they imply distinction or spatial relations that do not apply to God and His attributes.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, why is 'existing by His essence' (موجود بذاته) preferred over 'subsisting in His essence' (قائم بذاته) for God's knowledge?
A) Because 'subsistence' implies a physical location.
B) Because 'existence' is a literal term, while 'subsistence' is metaphorical when applied to attributes.
C) Because 'subsistence' implies that knowledge is separate from God.
D) Because the early scholars used 'existence'.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes exist by His essence without being distinct entities, so only literal terms like 'existing by His essence' should be used, not metaphorical ones like 'subsisting' or phrases implying spatial relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's knowledge, do you subconsciously picture it as something 'in' Him or 'with' Him, as if it were a separate entity? Will you now replace that image with the reality that His knowledge exists by His essence alone, without division or location?
?
The lesson warns against pious-sounding phrases that are metaphorically imprecise. Examine your own supplications: have you ever used terms about God's attributes that imply spatial or relational distance? Are you willing to refine your speech to align with divine transcendence, even if it feels less poetic?
?
If you truly believe that God's attributes are not distinct from His essence, how must this reshape your understanding of His knowledge? Can you honestly say that you rely on this truth when you pray, or do you still think of God's attributes as separate resources? What concrete step will you take this week to internalize this lesson's call to literal precision?

The Mu'tazilite Contradiction on Divine Names
Author critiques Mu'tazila for inconsistency in naming attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author exposes a logical inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite position on naming divine attributes.

Core Idea
Inconsistency in affirming names without meanings reveals flawed methodology.

This lesson sharpens our understanding of the debate over divine attributes. The author refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that their own principles lead to a contradiction: they affirm names for God even when the corresponding meaning cannot be established, while denying names in the observable world when the meaning is absent. Let's examine this argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation slowly. Notice the two groups mentioned: 'al-muqaddar' and the generality of the Mu'tazila.
The first group uses analogical reasoning improperly; the second group is the main target.
2
Identify the contradiction
The passage states that the Mu'tazila affirm names in the unseen (gha'ib) despite the impossibility of meaning, but deny names in the seen (shahid) when meaning is absent. Pinpoint this contrast.
3
Understand the analogy
Notice how the first group (al-muqaddar) analogizes divine attributes to created attributes like moving/stationary. The author rejects this as flawed.
This analogy treats God like contingent beings, which is inappropriate.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does this inconsistency matter? It shows that the Mu'tazila do not apply their own method consistently, undermining their entire approach to divine names.
This lesson highlights the importance of methodological consistency in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدر على المعنى عن الذات الذي يستحيل عليه المعنى، قياسا على اسم المتحرك والساكن والأسود والأبيض، وغير ذلك من الصفات وغيرهم من عامة المعتزلة ناقضوا فأثبتوا الاسم مع استحالة ثبوت المعنى في الغائب، ونفوا في الشاهد عند عدم المعنى . والله الموفق.
The one who measures the meaning from the essence upon which meaning is impossible, analogically to the name of moving and stationary and black and white, and other attributes; and others among the generality of the Mu'tazila contradicted, so they affirmed the name despite the impossibility of the meaning's establishment in the unseen, and denied it in the witnessed when the meaning is absent. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Mu'tazila regarding the naming of divine attributes. The author points out that they inconsistently affirm names (like 'knowing') for God even though the meaning (knowledge) cannot be established for the unseen without a proper basis, while in the case of the seen world they deny the name when the corresponding meaning is absent. The author highlights this contradiction as a flaw in their methodology.

Misconception: Mu'tazila always deny divine attribute names for God.
Correction: This passage shows they affirm names (e.g., Knowing) even when they cannot establish the meaning in the unseen, contradicting their own principles.

Checkpoint

A) They denied names and meanings altogether.
B) They affirmed names without meanings for the unseen but denied them in the seen when meaning is absent.
C) They affirmed both names and meanings for the unseen but denied them in the seen.
D) They denied the names but affirmed the meanings.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are inconsistent: they affirm divine names without establishing meanings in the unseen, yet deny names in the seen when meanings are absent.

?

Write down one specific inconsistency and take a step to align them.
?

Why 'Hulul' Cannot Apply to Divine Attributes
The author explains why 'hulul' is rejected for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of hulul (indwelling) and why it is impermissible to use this term for Allah's knowledge and attributes.

Core Idea
Hulul implies resting and settling, which cannot describe an attribute.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key theological distinction: the term 'hulul' (indwelling) is used for bodies, not attributes. Since divine attributes are not corporeal, applying 'hulul' to them is invalid, and even figurative use is avoided to prevent any suggestion of error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once silently, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms in Arabic.
Notice the definition of hulul given in the middle of the passage.
2
Understand the definition of hulul
The author defines hulul as sukun (resting) and istiqrar (settling). This definition is central to the argument.
Hulul is contrasted with irtihal (departure), showing it implies physical location.
3
Identify why attributes cannot be described by hulul
The passage states that an attribute (sifah) is not described as resting. Since hulul means resting, it cannot apply to any attribute, even corporeal ones like blackness in a body—except figuratively.
For corporeal attributes, figurative use is allowed, but for Allah it is prohibited.
4
Understand the prohibition even figuratively for Allah
The author emphasizes that for Allah's attributes, even figurative use of hulul is forbidden because it suggests error (wahm al-khata). This is a matter of theological caution.
This shows the strictness of Islamic theology in safeguarding divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن علم الباري عز وجل لا يقال : إنه حل ذاته، ولا يقال: ذاته محل للعلم ولما وراءه من الصفات إن كانت موجودة به لما أن الحلول هو السكون والاستقرار، يقال: حل فلان بمحل كذا وارتحل والمحلة السكن، والصفة لا توصف بالسكون ، وكذا هذا في صفات الأجسام، لا يقال: حل السواد الجسم إلا على التوسع، ولا يستعمل ذلك في صفاته تعالى على التوسع لوجوب الاجتناب في الخطأ حسب وجوب الاجتناب عما يوهم الخطأ.
Then know that it is not said of the knowledge of Allah, Mighty and Majestic, that it indwells (halla) His essence, nor is it said that His essence is a locus (mahall) for knowledge and other attributes if they exist by Him, because indwelling (hulul) is resting (sukun) and settling (istiqrar). It is said: 'So-and-so alighted at a place and departed,' and 'alighting place' is dwelling. And an attribute is not described as resting. And likewise this applies to the attributes of bodies: it is not said that blackness indwells the body except figuratively. And this is not used for His attributes - exalted is He - even figuratively, due to the necessity of avoiding error, just as it is necessary to avoid what suggests error.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of why the term 'hulul' (indwelling) cannot be applied to God's knowledge or attributes. The author defines hulul as resting or settling (sukun and istiqrar), which are properties of bodies. Attributes are not described as resting, so the term cannot apply. For corporeal attributes, saying 'blackness indwells the body' is only used figuratively, but for Allah, even figurative use is prohibited to avoid any suggestion of error. The author emphasizes the importance of avoiding even the illusion of error in speaking about Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'hulul' can be used for divine attributes as it is used for bodies.
Correction: The passage defines hulul as resting and settling, which applies only to bodies, not to attributes. For Allah, even figurative use is forbidden.

Checkpoint

A) Because knowledge is not a body.
B) Because hulul implies resting and settling, which cannot apply to an attribute.
C) Because the Mu'tazila used it.
D) Because the Quran forbids it.

Key Takeaway
The term 'hulul' cannot be used for Allah's attributes because it implies physical resting, and even figurative use is avoided to prevent error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah's attributes, are you careful to avoid any word that might imply physicality, even if it feels natural? Have you ever used a phrase like 'Allah's mercy descends' without considering the implication of direction? Will you now examine your speech for hidden anthropomorphisms and commit to more precise language?
?
This lesson says we must avoid even the suggestion of error. Think of a time you used a casual analogy for Allah (e.g., 'Allah's hands' without proper qualification). Does it align with this caution? Are you willing to change your language for the sake of tanzih, even if it feels unnatural?
?
The author stresses 'necessity of avoiding error'. Do you feel that same necessity in your own speech about Allah? Or are you comfortable with imprecise language because 'everyone knows what you mean'? What specific phrase will you stop using today to uphold this principle?

Appropriate Terminology for Divine Attributes: Subsistence vs. Existence
Al-Ash'ari contrasts 'qiyam' and 'wujud' for divine attributes and rejects relational descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari preferred the term 'wujud' (existence) over 'qiyam' (subsistence) for divine attributes and why relational expressions like 'in Him' or 'separate from Him' are rejected.

Core Idea
Literal terms like 'wujud' are preferred over metaphorical ones like 'qiyam' for divine attributes.

This lesson explores the precise language used to describe the relationship between God's attributes and His essence. Early theologians used 'qiyam' for subsistence, but al-Ash'ari argued that 'wujud' is more accurate because it is literal and avoids ambiguity. We will also examine why terms like 'with Him', 'in Him', 'adjacent', and 'separate' are considered inappropriate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the contrast between 'qiyam' and 'wujud' and the list of rejected expressions.
2
Identify the two key terms
Locate where al-Ash'ari distinguishes between 'qiyam' (subsistence) and 'wujud' (existence). Understand why he considers one metaphorical and the other literal.
The passage says 'qiyam' is majaz (metaphorical) and 'wujud' is haqiqa (literal).
3
Understand the rejection of relational expressions
Go through each rejected term: 'with Him', 'in Him', 'adjacent', 'separate'. Note the reason given for each rejection.
For example, 'with Him' is rejected because knowledge is not self-subsisting.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
The passage concludes that all these terms imply distinctness (taghayur), but there is no distinctness between God's essence and attributes. Reflect on why this principle is important.
This guards against anthropomorphism and maintains tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن متقدمي أصحاب الصفات يطلقون أن علمه قائم بذاته، وأبو الحسن الأشعري لم يرض بهذه العبارة وقال : إن علمه تعالى موجود بذاته تعالى لما أن لفظة القيام في الصفات مجاز ولفظة الوجود حقيقة، وأعلم أنه لا يقال إن علمه تعالى معه لأنه ليس بقائم بنفسه فيكون معه، ولا يقال: هو فيه لأنه تعالى ليس بظرف للعلم، والعلم أيضا ليس بمتمكن فيه، ولا يقال : إنه مجاور له لأنه غير مماس له لما أنه لا يقبل المماسة، ولا أنه مباين له لما أنه لا يقبل المفارقة، ولما أن هذه الألفاظ مستعملة في المتغايرات ولا تغاير فيما نحن فيه ووجوب الامتناع عن إطلاق هذه العبارات في مع الموصوفات في الشاهد للمعنى الأول دون الثاني.
Then indeed the early ones among the proponents of attributes (as-hab al-sifat) used to say that His knowledge subsists by His essence (qa'im bi-dhatihi). But Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari was not pleased with this expression and said: His knowledge - exalted is He - exists by His essence (mawjud bi-dhatihi), because the word 'subsistence' (qiyam) regarding the attributes is metaphorical (majaz) while the word 'existence' (wujud) is literal (haqiqa). And know that it is not said that His knowledge is 'with Him' (ma'ahu) because it is not self-subsisting (qa'im bi-nafsihi) so that it would be 'with Him'. Nor is it said that it is 'in Him' (fihi) because He - exalted is He - is not a receptacle (zarf) for knowledge, and knowledge is not located (mutamakkin) in Him. Nor is it said that it is 'adjacent to Him' (mujawir lahu) because it is not contiguous (mumas) with Him since He does not accept contiguity (mumassa). Nor that it is 'separate from Him' (mubayin lahu) because it does not accept separation (mufaraqa). And because these words are used for distinct things (mutaghayirat), but there is no distinctness (taghayur) in what we are discussing, and it is necessary to refrain from using these expressions regarding attributes just as one refrains in the witnessed case due to the first meaning (i.e., distinctness) but not the second (i.e., the actual reason of distinctness).
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage addresses the appropriate terminology for the relationship between God's attributes and His essence. The early theologians of the attributes used 'qiyam' (subsistence) to say that knowledge subsists by the essence, but al-Ash'ari preferred 'wujud' (existence) because 'qiyam' is metaphorical while 'wujud' is literal. Then the author lists several rejected expressions: 'with Him' (ma'ahu), 'in Him' (fihi), 'adjacent' (mujawir), and 'separate' (mubayin). Each is explained: knowledge is not 'with Him' because it is not self-subsisting; not 'in Him' because He is not a receptacle; not 'adjacent' because there is no contiguity; not 'separate' because there is no separation. All these terms imply otherness, but there is no otherness between the attribute and the essence in this context. The author concludes that such expressions must be avoided.

Key Terms
